Philosophers' Definition of Accident and Mental Existence
Al-Jurjani presents the philosophers' definition of accident and defends it against objections about mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of accident as an essence that in external existence is in a subject, how they address the problem of mental existence, and the role of actual existence in the definitions of substance and accident.

Core Idea
Accidents and substances are defined by their mode of existence in the external world, not in the mind.

The philosophers define accident as an essence that, when existing externally, inheres in a subject. But this raises a puzzle: if substance is defined by not being in a subject, what about substances in the mind? Al-Jurjani presents the philosophers' response and a subsequent critique, showing how the definitions hinge on actual external existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense and includes multiple layers of commentary.
2
Identify the definitions of substance and accident
Locate how the philosophers define substance and accident. Notice the key phrase 'an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is in a subject' for accident.
Substance is defined as 'existent not in a subject'.
3
Understand the mental existence objection
The objection is that if substance is defined as 'not in a subject', then mental images of substances would not be substances because they exist in the mind. Grasp why this is a problem.
The mind is considered a 'subject' in this context.
4
Examine the response and the role of actual existence
The philosophers respond by clarifying that the definition refers to external existence. Then follow the discussion about whether actual existence is required. Note the citation from Avicenna's al-Shifa.
The passage concludes that the author's definition is not correct because it omits the condition of actual existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما تعريفه عند الحكماء فماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت في موضوع أي في محل مقوم لما حل فيه. قوله : ( فماهية إذا وجدت إلخ ) اعلم أنَّه قسموا الموجود الممكن إلى الجوهر والعرض، وعرفوا الجوهر بالموجود لا في موضوع ولما ورد عليهم الإشكال بأنه يلزم أن لا تكون الجواهر في الذهن جواهر لكونها موجودة في موضوع مع أنَّ الجوهر جوهر سواءٌ نُسب إلى الإدراك العقلي أو إلى الوجود الخارجي قالوا: المراد ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج كانت لا في موضوع أي لا نعني به الشيء المحصل في الخارج الذي ليس في موضوع بل لو وجد لم يكن في موضوع سواء وجد في الخارج أو لا فالتعريف شامل لها ثم إنها أعراض أيضاً لكونها موجودة بالفعل في موضوع ولا منافاة بين كون الشيء الواحد جوهراً أو عرضاً بناء على أنَّ العرض هو الموجود في موضوع لا ما يكون في موضوع إذا وُجدت كذا قال المحقق الدواني في حواشيه القديمة في بحث الوجود الذهني وتبعه من جاء بعده ونص لعدم اشتراط الوجود بالفعل في الجوهر حتى قال بعضهم : إنَّ المركب الخيالي كجبل من ياقوت وبحر من زئبق لا شك في جوهريته إنما الشك في وجوده أقول : هذا مخالف لتصريحهم بأن المقسم الممكن الموجود إذ لا يمكن أن يراد به ما من شأنه أن يوجد في الخارج لأن كل ممكن كذلك فلا فائدة في التقييد ويستلزم بطلان انحصاره في القسمين إذ يصير القسمة هكذا الموجد . ثم قال : فيه ( ومعنى وجوده في كذا وإن كان يطلق ) أي قولنا وجد كذا في كذا إما بطريق الاشتراك أي الممكن إما أن يكون بحيث إذا وجد في الخارج كان لا في موضوع أو يكون موجوداً في الخارج في موضوع فيخرجُ ما لا يكون بالفعل في موضوع ويكون فيه إذا وجد كالسود المعدوم والحق أن الوجود بالفعل معتبر فيه كما هو المتبادر من قولهم الموجود لا في موضوع وتفسيرهم بماهية إذا وجدت ليس لأجل أن الوجود بالفعل ليس بمعتبر فيه بل الإشارة إلى زيادة الوجود ليخرج الواجب تعالى وإلى أنَّ المعتبر في الجوهرية كونه بهذه الصفة في الوجود الخارجي لا في العقل أنَّه ماهية إذا قيست إلى وجودها الخارجي ولوحظت بالنسبة إليه كانت لا في موضوع ولا شك أنَّ تلك الجواهر حال قيامها بالذهن يصدق عليها أنها موجودة في الخارج لا في موضوع وإن كانت باعتبار قيامها بالذهن في موضوع فهي جواهر وأعراض باعتبار القيام بالذهن وعدمه وكذا الحال في العرض وهذا هو المنصوص في الشفاء حيث قال : أما العلم فإنَّ فيه شبهة وذلك أن يقال : إِنَّ العلم هو المكتسب من صور الموجودات مجردة عن موادها وهي صور جواهر وأعراض، فإن كانت صور الأعراض أعراضاً ، فصور الجواهر كيف تكون أعراضاً فإن الجوهر لذاته جوهر، فماهيته لا تكون في موضوع البتة وماهيته محفوظة سواء نسبت إلى إدراك العقل لها أو نسبت إلى الوجود الخارجي فنقول : إنَّ ماهية الجَوْهَر جوهر بمعنى أنه الموجود في الأعيان لا في موضوع وهذه الصفة موجودة لماهية الجواهر المعقولة فإنها ماهية شأنها أن تكون موجودة في الأعيان لا في موضوع أي إِنَّ هذه الماهية معقولة عن أمر وجوده في الأعيان أن تكون لا في موضوع وأما وجوده في العقل بهذه الصفة فليس ذلك في حده من حيث أنه جوهر؛ أي ليس حد الجوهر أنه في العقل في موضوع بل حده أنّه سواء كان في العقل أو لم يكن، فإنَّ وجوده في الأعيان ليس في موضوع ثم قال : فإن قيل : فقد جعلتم ماهية الجوهر أنها تارة تكون جوهرا وتارة عرضا وقد منعتم هذا فنقول : أما منعنا أن يكون ماهية شيء يوجد في الأعيان مرة عرضاً ومرة جوهراً حتى يكون في الأعيان يحتاج إلى موضوع ما وفيها لا يحتاج إلى موضوع البتة ولم نمنع أن يكون معقول تلك الماهية يصير عرضاً انتهى كلامه وبما حررنا لك ظهر أنَّ الوجود بالفعل معتبر في الجوهر والعرض وأنَّ معنى الموجود في موضوع وماهية إذا وجدت كانت موضوعاً واحداً لا فرق بينهما إلا بالإجمال والتفصيل فلا يرد أن لا اختلاف في اعتبار الوجود بالفعل في تعريف العرض ولذا يستدلون بعدمية الوحدة وغيرها على عدم دخولها في العرض فتعريف المصنف ليس بصحيح.
As for its definition according to the philosophers: it is an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is in a subject, i.e., in a locus (maḥall) that constitutes what inheres in it. His saying: (an essence which, when it exists, etc.) Know that they divided the possible existent into substance and accident, and defined substance as the existent not in a subject. When the objection arose that then the substances in the mind would not be substances because they exist in a subject, whereas substance is substance whether related to intellectual perception or to external existence, they said: what is meant is an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is not in a subject; i.e., we do not mean the actualized thing in the external world that is not in a subject, but rather if it were to exist, it would not be in a subject, whether it exists externally or not. So the definition includes them. Then they are also accidents because they actually exist in a subject, and there is no contradiction between a single thing being a substance or an accident, based on the fact that accident is that which exists in a subject, not that which would be in a subject if it existed. This is what al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawānī said in his old glosses in the discussion of mental existence, and those after him followed. He stated that actual existence is not required in substance, until some said: a imaginary composite like a mountain of ruby and a sea of mercury – there is no doubt about its substantiality; the doubt is only about its existence. I say: this contradicts their explicit statement that the division is of possible existent (al-mumkin al-mawjūd), for it cannot be meant as that which has the potential to exist externally, because every possible is like that, so the restriction is useless, and it leads to the failure of the division into two categories, for the division would be: the existent... Then he said: In it ( and the meaning of its existence in something, although it is applied ) i.e., our saying 'found something in something' is either by way of homonymy, i.e., the possible either is such that when it exists externally it is not in a subject, or it exists externally in a subject. Thus what is not actually in a subject but would be in it if it existed, like non-existent blackness, is excluded. The truth is that actual existence is considered in it, as is the apparent meaning of their saying 'the existent not in a subject' and their interpretation as 'an essence which, when it exists' is not because actual existence is not considered, but rather it indicates the additional existence to exclude the Necessary, and that what is considered in substantiality is its being of this description in external existence, not in the intellect: that it is an essence which, when related to its external existence and observed in relation to it, is not in a subject. There is no doubt that those substances while subsisting in the intellect are truthfully described as being externally existent not in a subject, even though in terms of their subsistence in the intellect they are in a subject. So they are substances and accidents in terms of subsistence in the intellect and its absence. The same applies to accident. This is what is stated in al-Shifā', where he said: 'As for knowledge, there is a doubt in it, namely: it is said: knowledge is acquired from forms of existents stripped of their matter, and they are forms of substances and accidents. If forms of accidents are accidents, how can forms of substances be accidents? For substance is substance in itself, and its essence is never in a subject, and its essence is preserved whether related to the intellect's perception of it or to external existence. So we say: the essence of substance is substance meaning that it is the existent in concrete things not in a subject, and this description exists for the essence of intelligible substances, for they are essences that by nature exist in concrete things not in a subject. That is, this essence is abstracted from something whose existence in concrete things is not in a subject. As for its existence in the intellect with this description, that is not in its definition insofar as it is substance; i.e., the definition of substance is not that it is in the intellect in a subject, but its definition is that whether it is in the intellect or not, its existence in concrete things is not in a subject.' Then he said: 'If it is said: You have made the essence of substance sometimes be substance and sometimes accident, and you have prohibited this. We say: We only prohibited that the essence of a thing existing in concrete things be one time an accident and one time a substance, such that it needs a subject in concrete things and in them does not need a subject at all. We did not prohibit that the intellect's concept of that essence become an accident.' End of his speech. From what we have written for you, it appears that actual existence is considered in both substance and accident, and that the meaning of 'the existent in a subject' and 'an essence which, when it exists, is in a subject' are one, with no difference except between brevity and detail. So it is not objected that there is no difference in considering actual existence in the definition of accident, and therefore they infer from the absence of unity and other things that they do not fall under accident. Thus the author's definition is not correct.
Source pages p. 9

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' definition of accident as 'an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is in a subject'. The commentator then explains how the philosophers define substance and accident. They divide possible existents into substance (existent not in a subject) and accident (existent in a subject). An objection arises: if substance is defined as 'existent not in a subject', then substances in the mind (mental existents) would not be substances because they exist in the mind (which is a subject). The response is that the definition refers to the thing's nature when it exists externally; mental existence does not affect the definition. The commentator then discusses the view of al-Dawani and others who argue that actual external existence is not required for substantiality, giving the example of a imaginary mountain of ruby. He criticizes this view, saying it contradicts the division of possible existent because if potential existence is enough, then everything possible would be included, making the division useless. He then clarifies that actual existence is indeed considered. He cites from Avicenna's al-Shifa' to show that the essence of substance remains substance whether in the mind or externally, but when it is in the mind it has a mode of being that is accidental. The commentator concludes that the author's definition (of accident) is not correct because it does not consider actual existence.

Key Terms
العرض
الجوهر
الوجود الذهني
الشفاء
الممكن الموجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the definition of substance and accident does not require actual external existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that actual existence is considered; definitions are based on how the essence exists in the external world, not merely on potential or mental existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do the philosophers modify the definition of substance to 'an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is not in a subject'?
A) To exclude mental existents from being substances.
B) To include mental existents in the definition of substance.
C) To avoid the objection that mental substances would not be substances.
D) To make the definition applicable only to possible existents.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers define substance and accident based on their mode of external existence, not mental existence, and actual existence is essential to the definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about the attributes of Allah, do you treat them as mere mental concepts or do you affirm their actual existence in a way that changes how you live? How does distinguishing between mental and external existence affect your certainty about the reality of the Hereafter?
?
The passage argues that actual existence is essential to the definition of substance. In your own life, what 'substances' (core beliefs) do you hold only as mental ideas but not act upon? What would it mean for you to take them as actually existing in your external actions?
?
Avicenna says that the essence of substance remains substance even when conceived in the mind, but its mode becomes accidental. Are there truths about Allah that you have reduced to mere mental concepts, forgetting their external reality in worship? How can you bring that reality to bear on your daily prayer?

What Is Kalam?
advanced
8 min
Defining Substance and Accident in Kalam
Defines accident and substance based on existence in a subject, emphasizing actual existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Sunni kalam defines substance and accident, and why actual existence is critical to the definition.

Core Idea
Substance and accident are defined by their relation to a subject in actual external existence.

This passage from Sanusi's commentary establishes the foundational definitions of substance (jawhar) and accident (ʿarad) in Islamic kalam. The author carefully clarifies that these categories apply only to existents in the external world, not merely mental concepts. Before we can discuss attributes of Allah or creation, we must have precise shared terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall flow.
Notice the author's dense style; key terms are subject (موضوع), substance (جوهر), accident (عرض).
2
Identify the two categories
The passage classifies possible existents into two: those not in a subject (substance) and those in a subject (accident) when they exist externally.
The classification is based on external existence, not mental.
3
Understand the emphasis on actual existence
The author stresses that actual existence (الوجود بالفعل) is the criterion, not mere quiddity. This excludes non-existent things and ensures the definition applies to real existents.
This nuance prevents confusion with mental concepts.
4
Grasp the mental existence nuance
Substances in the mind are still considered substances externally, even though they are in the mind (accidentally). The same applies to accidents.
The classification is about external reality, not mental representation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممكن إما أن يكون بحيث إذا وجد في الخارج كان لافي موضوع أو يكون موجوداً في الخارج في موضوع فيخرجُ ما لا يكون بالفعل في موضوع ويكون فيه إذا وجد كالسود المعدوم والحق أن الوجود بالفعل معتبر فيه كما هو المتبادر من قولهم الموجود لا في موضوع وتفسيرهم بماهية إذا وجدت ليس لأجل أن الوجود بالفعل ليس بمعتبر فيه بل الإشارة إلى زيادة الوجود ليخرج الواجب تعالى وإلى أنَّ المعتبر في الجوهرية كونه بهذه الصفة في الوجود الخارجي لا في العقل أنَّه ماهية إذا قيست إلى وجودها الخارجي ولوحظت بالنسبة إليه كانت لا في موضوع ولا شك أنَّ تلك الجواهر حال قيامها بالذهن يصدق عليها أنها موجودة في الخارج لا في موضوع وإن كانت باعتبار قيامها بالذهن في موضوع فهي جواهر وأعراض باعتبار القيام بالذهن وعدمه وكذا الحال في العرض
The possible is either such that when it exists in the external world it is not in a subject, or it exists in the external world in a subject, so it excludes what is not actually in a subject and is in it when it exists, like the non-existent? Actually actual existence is considered therein, as is understood from their saying 'the existent not in a subject' and their interpretation of it as a quiddity if it exists — not because actual existence is not considered, but rather the indication is of the addition of existence to exclude the Necessary Exalted, and that what is considered in substantiality is its being with this attribute in external existence, not in the intellect, that it is a quiddity when measured to its external existence and observed relative to it, it is not in a subject. And there is no doubt that those substances, while they subsist in the mind, it is true of them that they exist in the external not in a subject, although by virtue of their subsistence in the mind they are in a subject, so they are substances and accidents by virtue of subsistence in the mind and its absence, and similarly the case for the accident.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage defines accident (عرض) and substance (جوهر) by their relation to a subject (موضوع). It begins by classifying possible existents: either when existing externally it is not in a subject (substance) or it is in a subject (accident). It then clarifies that actual existence (الوجود بالفعل) is considered in this classification, as indicated by the phrase 'the existent not in a subject'. The interpretation as 'a quiddity if it exists' is not to deny actual existence but to add existence so as to exclude God, and to emphasize that the criterion for substantiality is being such in external existence, not in the mind. The passage then addresses a potential confusion: substances in the mind are still truly 'existents not in a subject' in the external sense, even though they are in the mind considered as accidents relative to the mind. The same analysis applies to accidents. Thus, the definition relies on actual external existence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of substance and accident applies to things in the mind as well as the external world.
Correction: The author clarifies that the criterion is actual external existence; being in the mind does not affect the external classification.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the defining characteristic of a substance (جوهر) when it exists externally?
A) It is always in a subject.
B) It is not in a subject.
C) It is only in the mind.
D) It is composed of matter and form.

Key Takeaway
Substance is that which, when existing externally, is not in a subject; accident is that which exists externally in a subject, and actual existence is essential for the classification.

This lesson teaches that actual external existence is what matters for being a substance or accident, not just mental concepts. How will you step into actual existence today?
?
Every accident depends on a subject. Reflect on your own qualities: your intelligence, your wealth, your health. Are they accidents that depend on something else? What does this tell you about your absolute dependence on Allah, the only Self-Subsisting? How can you make this dependence a living reality, not just a concept?

Does it subsist independently, or is it a quality of something else? How does your answer shape your understanding of your relationship with your Creator?

The Doubt from al-Shifa on Mental Images of Substances
Resolves a doubt about mental images of substances

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a doubt about mental images of substances is resolved by distinguishing between quiddity and existence, and why actual existence must be considered in definitions of substance and accident.

Core Idea
Substance remains substance in the mind; mental existence does not change its quiddity.

This passage from al-Shifa presents a clever doubt: if knowledge consists of forms of things, and forms of accidents are accidents, then forms of substances would become accidents—contradicting their essence. The resolution hinges on distinguishing between the quiddity's inherent attribute (not being in a subject) and its mental existence. We'll follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the doubt and the response.
Pay attention to the distinction between quiddity and existence.
2
Identify the doubt
The doubt: if mental forms of substances exist in the mind (a subject), they become accidents. But substance cannot be accident.
3
Understand the response
The response: the quiddity of substance is 'not in a subject' by definition. Mental existence does not change that attribute; it only applies to the concept.
The definition of substance does not require it to be not in a subject in the mind.
4
Consider the criticism of al-Iji
The passage concludes that actual existence must be considered in both substance and accident, so al-Iji's definition is incorrect because it ignores this.
This connects to broader debates about definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا هو المنصوص في الشفاء حيث قال : أما العلم فإنَّ فيه شبهة وذلك أن يقال : إِنَّ العلم هو المكتسب من صور الموجودات مجردة عن موادها وهي صور جواهر وأعراض، فإن كانت صور الأعراض أعراضاً ، فصور الجواهر كيف تكون أعراضاً فإن الجوهر لذاته جوهر، فماهيته لا تكون في موضوع البتة وماهيته محفوظة سواء نسبت إلى إدراك العقل لها أو نسبت إلى الوجود الخارجي فنقول : إنَّ ماهية الجَوْهَر جوهر بمعنى أنه الموجود في الأعيان لا في موضوع وهذه الصفة موجودة لماهية الجواهر المعقولة فإنها ماهية شأنها أن تكون موجودة في الأعيان لا في موضوع أي إِنَّ هذه الماهية معقولة عن أمر وجوده في الأعيان أن تكون لا في موضوع وأما وجوده في العقل بهذه الصفة فليس ذلك في حده من حيث أنه جوهر؛ أي ليس حد الجوهر أنه في العقل في موضوع بل حده أنّه سواء كان في العقل أو لم يكن، فإنَّ وجوده في الأعيان ليس في موضوع ثم قال : فإن قيل : فقد جعلتم ماهية الجوهر أنها تارة تكون جوهرا وتارة عرضا وقد منعتم هذا فنقول : أما منعنا أن يكون ماهية شيء يوجد في الأعيان مرة عرضاً ومرة جوهراً حتى يكون في الأعيان يحتاج إلى موضوع ما وفيها لا يحتاج إلى موضوع البتة ولم نمنع أن يكون معقول تلك الماهية يصير عرضاً انتهى كلامه وبما حررنا لك ظهر أنَّ الوجود بالفعل معتبر في الجوهر والعرض وأنَّ معنى الموجود في موضوع وماهية إذا وجدت كانت موضوعاً واحداً لا فرق بينهما إلا بالإجمال والتفصيل فلا يرد أن لا اختلاف في اعتبار الوجود بالفعل في تعريف العرض ولذا يستدلون بعدمية الوحدة وغيرها على عدم دخولها في العرض فتعريف المصنف ليس بصحيح
And this is what is stated in al-Shifa where he said: As for knowledge, there is a doubt in it, and that is that it is said: Knowledge is acquired from forms of existents stripped of their matters, and they are forms of substances and accidents. If the forms of accidents are accidents, then how can forms of substances be accidents? For the substance by its essence is substance, so its quiddity is not in a subject at all, and its quiddity is preserved whether it is related to the intellect's perception of it or related to external existence. So we say: The quiddity of substance is substance in the sense that it is that which exists in external things not in a subject, and this attribute exists for the quiddity of intelligible substances, for it is a quiddity whose nature is to exist in external things not in a subject, i.e., this quiddity is conceived from something whose existence in external things is that it is not in a subject. As for its existence in the intellect with this attribute, that is not in its definition insofar as it is substance; i.e., the definition of substance is not that it is in the intellect in a subject, rather its definition is that whether it is in the intellect or not, its existence in external things is not in a subject. Then he said: If it is said: You have made the quiddity of substance sometimes substance and sometimes accident, but you have forbidden this. So we say: We have only forbidden that the quiddity of a thing exists in external things once as accident and once as substance, such that in external things it needs a subject and in them does not need a subject at all. But we have not forbidden that the concept of that quiddity becomes an accident. End of his speech. And by what we have set forth for you, it appears that actual existence is considered in substance and accident, and that the meaning of 'existent in a subject' and 'quiddity if it exists was in a subject' are one, with no difference between them except in terms of brevity and detail. So it cannot be objected that there is no difference in considering actual existence in the definition of accident, and therefore they argue by the absence of unity and other things to show that it does not enter into accident. So the author's definition is not correct.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage presents and resolves a doubt (شبهة) from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. The doubt concerns whether mental images of substances, which exist in the mind (a subject), become accidents. The resolution is that the quiddity of a substance remains inherently not in a subject even when conceived in the mind; its existence in the intellect does not alter its essential definition. The passage then concludes that actual existence is indeed considered in both substance and accident, and criticizes the author's (al-Iji's) definition for being inaccurate due to neglecting this nuance.

ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mental forms of substances are accidents because they exist in the mind.
Correction: The quiddity of substance is defined by not being in a subject in external existence; mental existence does not change that essential attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the key conclusion from the citation of al-Shifa regarding the definition of accident?
A) Mental forms of substances are accidents.
B) Actual existence is not considered in the definition of accident.
C) The definition of substance and accident must consider actual external existence.
D) The author's definition is correct.

Key Takeaway
The definition of substance as 'not in a subject' applies to its external existence, not its mental existence; thus, mental images of substances do not become accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How the Subject Constitutes Its Accident: The Role of Hyle
Al-Sanusi explains the need to interpret 'subject' as 'its subject' to include hyle-borne accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject of an accident is interpreted as 'its subject' (موضوعه) to include accidents that inhere in hyle (prime matter).

Core Idea
The subject of an accident is a constituent of that accident—but 'subject' here means the immediate subject, not a remote one.

This passage from al-Sanusi's commentary clarifies a key phrase in the definition of the subject of an accident. The phrase 'constituent of what inheres in it' might seem straightforward, but it raises a problem: what about accidents that inhere in hyle? Al-Sanusi explains that by specifying 'its subject', the definition includes such accidents while excluding form. Let's explore this subtle point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Notice the technical terms: مقوم, موضوع, هيولى, صورة.
2
Identify the problem with hyle
The passage says accidents in hyle would be excluded if 'subject' is taken literally. Why? Because hyle itself is constituted by the accident? Actually, hyle is a constituent of the accident. Understand the subtlety.
The gloss reinterprets 'subject' as 'its subject' to avoid exclusion.
3
Understand the solution: 'its subject'
By 'its subject' they mean the immediate subject of the accident, which includes hyle but excludes form. Reflect on why form is not included.
Form inheres in hyle, but the accident inheres in the composite, not directly in form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( مقوم لما حل فيه الظاهر مقوم لها ولذا قالوا : المراد بموضوع موضوعه لئلا يخرج الأعراض القائمة بالهيولى فإنها موجودة في محل متقوم بما حل فيه ولا يدخل الصورة إذ يصدق عليها أنها موجودة في محل مقوم للأعراض الحالة لكون الهيولى مقومة للأعراض الحالة فيها . جواهر كما اعترض السالمي وإن قدر المضاف أي ذو ماهية يلزم أن لا تكون الجواهر الكلية جواهر إلا أن يختار الأول ولا يعتبر الكلية في الماهية بل يراد بها ما به الشيء هو هو جزئياً كان أو كلياً أو يختار الثاني ويكتفي في النسبة بذو بالمغايرة الاعتبارية
His saying: (a constituent of what inheres in it) ostensibly a constituent of it, and therefore they said: by 'subject' is meant its subject, so that the accidents subsisting in hyle are not excluded, for they exist in a place constituted by what inheres in it, and the form does not enter because it is true of it that it exists in a place that is a constituent of the accidents that inhere, because hyle is a constituent of the accidents inhering in it. Substances as the Salimi objected? And if the genitive construction is assumed, i.e., 'possessor of a quiddity', then it would follow that universal substances are not substances, unless one chooses the first meaning and does not consider universality in the quiddity, but rather intends by it that by which the thing is itself, whether particular or universal, or one chooses the second and suffices in the relation with 'possessor' by conceptual difference.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a commentary gloss on the phrase 'مقوم لما حل فيه' (a constituent of what inheres in it). It explains that the subject (موضوع) is a constituent of the accident that inheres in it, but this is understood in a specific sense: the subject's own subject (موضوعه) is what is meant, to avoid excluding accidents that inhere in hyle (هيولى). Such accidents exist in a subject (hyle) that itself is constituted by the accident? Actually, the gloss clarifies that hyle is a constituent of the accidents inhering in it. Then it addresses an objection by al-Salimi: if we interpret 'subject' as 'possessor of a quiddity', then universal substances would not be substances. The resolution is to either not consider universality in the quiddity (i.e., the quiddity is what makes a thing itself, whether particular or universal) or to accept the second interpretation with a conceptual distinction.

Key Terms
مقوم
موضوع
هيولى
صورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Sanusi specify 'its subject' in the definition?
A) To include accidents that inhere in hyle.
B) To exclude universal substances from being substances.
C) To show that the subject is a part of the accident.
D) To prove that hyle is not a subject.

Key Takeaway
The subject of an accident is its immediate subject, like hyle for accidents inhering in it, not a remote subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the accidents of your own life—your status, wealth, relationships—do you treat them as defining you (as if you are a hyle constituted by them) or as mere attachments to your unchanging essence? Which interpretation is more honest?
?
The passage insists on precision: 'subject' must mean 'its subject'. Think of a time when a misunderstanding arose because you didn't distinguish between the immediate and remote cause of something. How can you apply this lesson to better understand the true causes of your actions?
?
If hyle is real but never encountered without form, are there 'invisible' realities in your own soul that only become apparent through their effects? How will you strive to know the immediate subject of your own spiritual state today?

Al-Salimi's Objection and the Universality of Substances
Al-Sanusi addresses al-Salimi's objection regarding universal substances and proposes two resolutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand an objection by al-Salimi about universal substances and how it can be resolved by reinterpreting 'subject' as 'possessor of a quiddity'.

Core Idea
Universal substances remain substances if 'quiddity' means what makes a thing itself, regardless of universality.

Al-Sanusi's commentary does not end with the clarification about hyle. He then addresses an objection from al-Salimi. If we interpret 'subject' as 'possessor of a quiddity' (ذو ماهية), then universal substances—which have a universal quiddity—would not be substances. Al-Sanusi presents two ways to avoid this problem. Let's see how this subtle issue is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and English. Note the technical terms: جواهر, السالمي, ماهية, كلية.
This is an advanced metaphysical discussion.
2
Understand the objection
The objection is that if 'subject' means 'possessor of a quiddity', then universal substances (e.g., 'animal' as a universal) would not be substances because their quiddity is universal. Grasp why this is problematic.
Substances are typically individual, not universal.
3
Analyze the two resolutions
Two resolutions are given: (1) Do not consider universality as part of quiddity; quiddity is simply 'what makes a thing itself'. (2) Accept the interpretation but with a conceptual difference in the relation. Understand how each preserves the substantiality of universal substances.
The first resolution is more common; the second is an alternative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( مقوم لما حل فيه الظاهر مقوم لها ولذا قالوا : المراد بموضوع موضوعه لئلا يخرج الأعراض القائمة بالهيولى فإنها موجودة في محل متقوم بما حل فيه ولا يدخل الصورة إذ يصدق عليها أنها موجودة في محل مقوم للأعراض الحالة لكون الهيولى مقومة للأعراض الحالة فيها . جواهر كما اعترض السالمي وإن قدر المضاف أي ذو ماهية يلزم أن لا تكون الجواهر الكلية جواهر إلا أن يختار الأول ولا يعتبر الكلية في الماهية بل يراد بها ما به الشيء هو هو جزئياً كان أو كلياً أو يختار الثاني ويكتفي في النسبة بذو بالمغايرة الاعتبارية
His saying: (a constituent of what inheres in it) ostensibly a constituent of it, and therefore they said: by 'subject' is meant its subject, so that the accidents subsisting in hyle are not excluded, for they exist in a place constituted by what inheres in it, and the form does not enter because it is true of it that it exists in a place that is a constituent of the accidents that inhere, because hyle is a constituent of the accidents inhering in it. Substances as the Salimi objected? And if the genitive construction is assumed, i.e., 'possessor of a quiddity', then it would follow that universal substances are not substances, unless one chooses the first meaning and does not consider universality in the quiddity, but rather intends by it that by which the thing is itself, whether particular or universal, or one chooses the second and suffices in the relation with 'possessor' by conceptual difference.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a commentary gloss on the phrase 'مقوم لما حل فيه' (a constituent of what inheres in it). It explains that the subject (موضوع) is a constituent of the accident that inheres in it, but this is understood in a specific sense: the subject's own subject (موضوعه) is what is meant, to avoid excluding accidents that inhere in hyle (هيولى). Such accidents exist in a subject (hyle) that itself is constituted by the accident? Actually, the gloss clarifies that hyle is a constituent of the accidents inhering in it. Then it addresses an objection by al-Salimi: if we interpret 'subject' as 'possessor of a quiddity', then universal substances would not be substances. The resolution is to either not consider universality in the quiddity (i.e., the quiddity is what makes a thing itself, whether particular or universal) or to accept the second interpretation with a conceptual distinction.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Universal substances are not substances because they are universal.
Correction: The passage shows that quiddity can be understood without universality, so universal substances remain substances.

A) Accidents in hyle are excluded.
B) Universal substances are not substances.
C) Form becomes a subject.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
Universal substances are still substances because quiddity can be understood as 'what makes a thing itself' without regard to universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, you often define who you are by specific traits or roles. But are those traits truly part of your 'quiddity'—what makes you you—or are they accidental? Al-Sanusi warns us not to confuse universality with essence. Take one attribute you consider central to your identity. How would your self-understanding change if you discovered it was not essential? Can you still be you without it?
?
Al-Sanusi faced a logical contradiction and resolved it by refining definitions. Think of a time when you encountered a contradiction in your faith or understanding of Allah. Did you dismiss it, or did you dig deeper into the meanings? What does your reaction reveal about your trust that truth is coherent? Commit today to approach one difficult theological question with the same precision as al-Sanusi, not avoiding it but seeking clarity.

Meanings of 'Existence in Something' and Accident Definition
Distinguishes various meanings of 'existence in something' and defines the specific meaning for accident as identity with being in a subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different meanings of 'existence in something' and the specific definition of accident as the identity of its existence with being in a subject.

Core Idea
An accident's existence in itself is identical to its existence in a subject.

The phrase 'existence in something' can mean many things. Sanusi enumerates several meanings before isolating the one that defines an accident: its existence in itself is exactly its existence in the subject. This passage clarifies a key term in kalam ontology and warns against a common confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the list of meanings
The passage opens by listing different meanings of 'existence in something'. Find each one and note how they differ from the specific meaning for accident.
The list includes part/whole, universal/particular, body in place/time, thing in health/sickness/happiness.
3
Isolate the specific meaning for accident
Sanusi then defines the specific meaning relevant to accident: 'its existence is its existence in the subject' such that they are indistinguishable in sensory reference.
This is called 'حلول' (indwelling).
4
Understand the warning
The passage warns against a misunderstanding: one might think that an accident's self-existence is the same as its existence in the subject. Sanusi refutes this by noting that one can say 'it exists in itself, then it subsists in the body', showing a real distinction.
The possibility of self-subsistence differs from subsistence for another.
5
Read the definition of substance
Finally, the passage defines substance as a quiddity that when existing externally is not in a subject. This contrasts with accident and completes the picture.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو الحقيقة والمجاز ( على معان مختلفة ) كوجود الجزء في الكل والكلي في الجزئي وكوجود الجسم في المكان أو الزمان ومثل كون الشيء في الصحة أو المرض وكونه في السعادة ( أن يكون وجوده هو وجوده في الموضوع بحيث لا يتمايزان في الإشارة الحسية كما مر في تفسير الحلول وقد يتوهم من هذه العبارة أن وجود السواد في نفسه مثلا هو وجوده في الجسم وقيامه به وليس بشيء إذ يصح أن يقال وجد في نفسه فقام بالجسم ولا يخفى أن إمكان ثبوت شيء في نفسه غير إمكان ثبوته لغيره وعرفوا الجوهر بأنه ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج لم تكن في موضوع وإن جاز أن يكون في
Or it is literal and metaphorical (in different meanings), such as the existence of the part in the whole, the universal in the particular, the existence of the body in place or time, and like a thing being in health or sickness and its being in happiness. (That its existence is its existence in the subject such that they do not differ in sensory indication, as passed in the explanation of indwelling. And one might be misled by this expression into thinking that the existence of blackness in itself, for example, is its existence in the body and its subsistence by it, and it is nothing, for it is correct to say: it exists in itself, then it subsists in the body. And it is not hidden that the possibility of something's subsistence in itself is different from the possibility of its subsistence for another. And they defined substance as a quiddity that when it exists in the external is not in a subject, although it is possible that it be in...
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage clarifies the various meanings of the phrase 'existence in something' (وجود في كذا) and isolates the one relevant to accident. It lists meanings: part in whole, universal in particular, body in place or time, thing in health. The specific meaning for accident is that its existence in itself is identical to its existence in the subject, such that they are indistinguishable in sensory reference, called 'حلول' (indwelling). The passage then warns against a misunderstanding: one might think that the existence of an accident (like blackness) in itself is the same as its existence in the body and its subsistence by it. This is refuted because one can say it exists in itself and then subsists in the body, indicating a real distinction. The possibility of self-subsistence differs from subsistence for another. Finally, it defines substance as a quiddity that when existing externally is not in a subject.

Key Terms
وجود في كذا
حلول
عرض
جوهر
ماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The accident's existence in itself is identical to its existence in the subject, so they are one and the same.
Correction: The passage insists they are distinct though inseparable; one can say 'exists in itself, then subsists in the body', showing a real distinction. The possibility of self-subsistence differs from subsistence for another.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which meaning of 'existence in something' applies to accidents (أعراض)?
A) Part in whole.
B) Universal in particular.
C) Existence of body in place.
D) Existence whose existence in itself is its existence in the subject.

Key Takeaway
The defining characteristic of an accident is that its existence in itself is the same as its existence in the subject, with no distinction in sensory indication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a strong emotion like anger, do you identify yourself with that anger, or do you recognize it as an accident subsisting in you? How does the lesson's distinction between self-existence and existence in a subject help you detach from fleeting states?
?
The passage warns against conflating the accident's self-existence with its existence in the body. How often do you conflate your own worth (your substance) with temporary accidents like success, health, or failure? What step can you take today to remember your substance is not defined by these accidents?
?
If your soul is a substance and not an accident, what does that imply about your ultimate identity and accountability? Will you live today as a substance defined by its Creator, or let accidental circumstances dictate your self-worth?

Definition of 'Arad: Existence in the Subject
Definition of 'arad with examples of various meanings of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'arad (accident) in kalam and the various meanings of 'existence in' a subject.

Core Idea
An accident's existence is its very inherence in a subject — no separate self-existence.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of accident ('arad) as presented in the matn. The author begins by stating that an accident is that whose existence in itself is identical to its existence in the subject, such that they cannot be separated in sensory indication. Various analogies are given to clarify different senses of 'existence in' something. Understanding this definition is crucial for distinguishing accidents from substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage defining 'arad
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the definition and the examples of different meanings of 'existence in.'
The definition is given in the form of an explanation of reality and metaphor.
2
Examine the examples of existence
The author lists several types: part in whole, universal in particular, body in place, thing in state. Reflect on how these examples illustrate the concept.
Not all examples are the same as an accident's existence in a subject.
3
Understand the key phrase: 'not distinguished in sensory indication'
This phrase is central to the definition. It means that the existence of the accident and its inherence are not two separate things in perception.
This will be elaborated in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : تعريف العرض أو الحقيقة والمجاز ( على معان مختلفة ) كوجود الجزء في الكل والكلي في الجزئي وكوجود الجسم في المكان أو الزمان ومثل كون الشيء في الصحة أو المرض وكونه في السعادة ( أن يكون وجوده هو وجوده في الموضوع بحيث لا يتمايزان في الإشارة الحسية كما مر في تفسير الحلول وقد يتوهم من هذه العبارة أن وجود السواد في نفسه مثلا هو وجوده في الجسم وقيامه به وليس بشيء إذ يصح أن يقال وجد في نفسه فقام بالجسم ولا يخفى أن إمكان ثبوت شيء في نفسه غير إمكان ثبوته لغيره وعرفوا الجوهر بأنه ماهية إذا وجدت في الخارج لم تكن في موضوع وإن جاز أن يكون في قوله : ( ومثل كون الشيء في (الصحة) أي كونه في حال من أحواله . قوله : ( لا يتمايزان) أي تحقيقاً أو تقديراً وتحقيق ذلك أن ملاقاة موجود لموجود بالتمام لا على سبيل المماسة والمجاورة بل بحيث لا يكون بينهما تباين في الوضع ويحصل للثاني صفة من الأول كملاقاة السواد للجسم يسمى حلولاً والموجود الأول حالاً والثاني محلاً كذا في شرح المقاصد . قوله : ( وقد يتوهم إلخ ) رد لما في شرح العقائد للمحقق التفتازاني حيث قال : ومعنى وجود العرض في الموضوع هو أن يكون وجوده في نفسه هو وجوده في الموضوع ولذا يمتنع الانتقال عليه لكنه موافق لما نقله المحقق الدواني في حواشيه عن تعليقات الشيخ من أن وجود الأعراض في أنفسها هو وجوداتها في موضوعاتها قوله : ( إذ يصح أن يقال إلخ ) فالقيام متأخر بالذات عن وجوده في نفسه وفيه أنا لا نسلم صحة هذا القول كيف وقد قلتم : إنَّ الموضوع شرط لوجود العرض فلو كان الوجود متقدماً على القيام لم يكن الموضوع محتاجا إليه ولو سلم فليكف للترتب بالفاء التغاير الاعتباري كما في قولهم : رماه فقتله . قوله : ( ولا يخفى أنَّ إمكان إلخ ) دليل ثان على التغاير وحاصلة أن إمكان الوجود الرابطي مغاير لإمكان الوجود المحمولي لتحقق الأول في الأمور الاعتبارية القائمة بمحالها كالعمى والثاني في الذوات القائمة بنفسها فيكون الوجودان أيضاً متغايرين وفيه أنَّ التغاير بين الإمكانين في العرض ممنوع وثبوته فيما عداه لا يجدي نفعاً إذ المتوهم يقول أنَّ وجوده في نفسه هو وجوده في الموضوع. قوله: (وإن جاز إلخ) يعنى أنَّ نفي الكون في الموضوع أعم من أن لا يكون في محل كذا لا وجوده المذكور سابقاً لأنَّ الضمير في وجوده راجع إلى العروض وليست المعاني المختلفة كلها كوجود العرض في المحل كما لا يخفى . قوله : (إذ يصح أن يقال إلخ ) هذا لا يفيد التغاير الحقيقي الذي هو المطلوب إنما المفيد له هو قوله : ولا يخفى إلخ فهو دليل مستقل على المطلوب.
The first observatory - the first aim: Definition of accident. Or reality and metaphor (with various meanings) such as the existence of a part in the whole and the universal in the particular, and like the existence of body in place or time, and like a thing being in health or illness and its being in happiness (that its existence is its existence in the subject such that they are not distinguished in sensory indication, as passed in the interpretation of indwelling). And one might misunderstand from this expression that the existence of blackness in itself, for example, is its existence in the body and its inherence in it, and it is nothing; for it is correct to say: it existed in itself then it inhered in the body. And it is not hidden that the possibility of something's subsistence in itself is other than the possibility of its subsistence in another. And they defined substance as an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is not in a subject, even if it may be in [a place]. His saying: (And like a thing being in [health]) i.e. its being in one of its states. His saying: (they are not distinguished) i.e. in actuality or in estimation, and the verification of that is that the contact of an existent with an existent completely, not by way of touch or adjacency but such that there is no separation in position between them, and a quality from the first occurs to the second, like the contact of blackness with body, is called indwelling, and the first existent is called 'indweller' and the second 'locus' — thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. His saying: (And one might misunderstand, etc.) is a refutation of what is in Sharh al-'Aqa'id of al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani where he said: The meaning of the existence of an accident in the subject is that its existence in itself is its existence in the subject, and therefore it cannot be transferred. But it agrees with what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani transmitted in his glosses from the comments of the Shaykh: that the existence of accidents in themselves is their existences in their subjects. His saying: (For it is correct to say, etc.) — so inherence is posterior in essence to its existence in itself. And in it [i.e., this argument] we do not concede the correctness of this statement; how [can it be] when you have said that the subject is a condition for the existence of the accident? For if existence preceded inherence, the subject would not be needed. And even if conceded, it would suffice for the ordering by the letter 'fa'' [indicating immediate sequence] the conceptual distinctness, as in their saying: 'He shot him and killed him.' His saying: (And it is not hidden that possibility, etc.) is a second proof of distinctness, and its summary is that the possibility of connective existence is different from the possibility of predicative existence, since the first is realized in conceptual matters subsisting by their loci, like blindness, and the second in essences subsisting by themselves; thus the two existences are also different. And in it [i.e., this argument]: the distinctness between the two possibilities is denied in the case of the accident, and its affirmation elsewhere is of no benefit, for the one misunderstanding says that its existence in itself is its existence in the subject. His saying: (And even if it is permissible, etc.) means that the negation of being-in-a-subject is more general than that it should not be in a locus — that is, not its existence mentioned previously, because the pronoun in 'its existence' refers to accidentalness, and the various meanings are not all like the existence of accident in the locus, as is not hidden. His saying: (For it is correct to say, etc.) This does not benefit the real distinctness which is sought; rather what benefits it is his saying: 'And it is not hidden, etc.' — so it is an independent proof for the sought.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of 'arad (accident). It begins by presenting the matn definition: 'arad is that whose existence in itself is identical to its existence in the subject, such that they are not distinguishable in sensory indication. The commentary then lists various meanings of 'existence' (e.g., part in whole, universal in particular, body in place, thing in a state like health). It then raises a misunderstanding: someone might think that the existence of blackness in itself is exactly its existence in the body and its inherence, which would make inherence unnecessary. The author refutes this by noting that we can say 'it existed in itself, then it inhered in the body,' indicating priority. Moreover, the possibility of something's subsistence in itself is different from its possibility in another. The commentary then explains the phrase 'they are not distinguished' in terms of actual or estimative non-distinction, and defines 'indwelling' (hulul) as the contact of an existent with another such that a quality accrues. It then addresses the objection from al-Taftazani's Sharh al-'Aqa'id, who held that the existence of an accident in the subject is precisely its existence in itself. The author responds that this would make the subject unnecessary, and that inherence is posterior. He also provides a second proof based on the difference between 'connective existence' (al-wujud al-ribati) and 'predicative existence' (al-wujud al-mahmuli), and their corresponding possibilities. Finally, he clarifies that the negation of being in a subject does not entail the absence of any locus, and that the various meanings of 'existence' are not all like the existence in a locus. The passage concludes by noting that the phrase 'for it is correct to say' does not prove real distinctness; rather the independent proof is the argument from possibility.

عرض
وجود
موضوع
حلول

Checkpoint
According to the definition in this passage, what does it mean for an accident to exist in a subject?
A) The accident is spatially located inside the subject.
B) The accident's existence in itself is the same as its existence in the subject.
C) The accident exists separately from the subject.
D) The accident is a part of the subject.

Key Takeaway
An accident is defined as that whose existence is identical to its inherence in a subject, with no separate self-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often define yourself by your qualities—your health, your happiness, your intelligence. But this lesson says those accidents have no existence of their own; they are only 'in' you. Does that make you feel like your worth is attached to something that fades? Or could it free you to see your core self as something that simply receives? In light of this, what would it mean for you to base your identity on something that does not change or vanish?
?
The passage lists many ways one thing can 'exist in' another: a part in a whole, a universal in a particular, a body in place, a state in a person. Look at your own life right now: what are you 'in'—your job, a relationship, a mood? Are you treating those as if they are your very existence, like an accident that cannot be separated? How might acknowledging your existence as something that is not identical to those attachments change how you handle losing them?

Refuting the Misunderstanding: The Distinction Between Self-Existence and Inherence
Refutation of the misunderstanding that accident's self-existence is identical to its inherence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific misunderstanding about blackness and the first argument refuting it by showing the priority of self-existence over inherence.

Core Idea
Blackness exists in itself first, then inheres — showing they are distinct.

The author now addresses a potential misunderstanding of the definition: one might think that the existence of an accident like blackness in itself is exactly its existence in the body, making inherence redundant. The author refutes this by noting that we can say 'it existed in itself, then it inhered in the body,' indicating temporal or logical priority. This lesson explains that argument and the subsequent discussion about the nature of inherence and the refutation of al-Taftazani's view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misunderstanding
Read the passage starting from 'وقد يتوهم' and note what exactly is being misunderstood about blackness.
The misunderstanding is that blackness's existence in itself is its existence in the body.
2
Understand the refutation: precedence
The author argues that we can say 'it existed in itself then it inhered' showing that self-existence and inherence are distinct.
The word 'then' implies sequence, even if not temporal.
3
Examine the objection and response
An objector might say that the subject is a condition for existence, so self-existence cannot precede. The response uses the concept of conceptual distinctness.
See the reply about 'رماه فقتله'.
4
Clarify the meaning of 'not distinguished' and refute Taftazani
The author explains that 'not distinguished' means in actuality or estimation, and defines indwelling as contact without separation. Then he directly refutes al-Taftazani's view.
This reinforces the definition and answers a major objection.

Commentary

The author responds that this would make the subject unnecessary, and that inherence is posterior. He also provides a second proof based on the difference between 'connective existence' (al-wujud al-ribati) and 'predicative existence' (al-wujud al-mahmuli), and their corresponding possibilities. Finally, he clarifies that the negation of being in a subject does not entail the absence of any locus, and that the various meanings of 'existence' are not all like the existence in a locus. The passage concludes by noting that the phrase 'for it is correct to say' does not prove real distinctness; rather the independent proof is the argument from possibility.

حلول

تغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence of an accident in itself is identical to its existence in the subject, so it does not need a subject.
Correction: They are distinct; the accident exists in itself in a way that is prior to inherence, and the subject is necessary for its actualization.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument the author uses to show that blackness's existence in itself is different from its existence in the body?
A) Blackness cannot exist without a body.
B) One can say 'it existed then inhered' indicating priority.
C) The body and blackness are distinct substances.
D) Inherence is a separate quality.

Key Takeaway
The self-existence of an accident is conceptually prior to its inherence, refuting the claim that they are identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that even the blackness of a thing has its own existence before it inheres in a body. Reflect on your own character: are there habits or sins you have come to see as 'just who you are' rather than accidents that inhere in you? How does this conceptual distinction free you to repent and change without despairing? Commit to identifying one trait you have fused with your identity and ask Allah to help you see it as distinct, so you can work on it.
?
The author refutes the view that the existence of an accident in itself is identical to its existence in the subject. Do you ever act as if your anger, pride, or lust is truly 'you' rather than something that inheres in you? If you believed they were merely accidents, would you feel more accountable to discipline them? Write down one instance where you excused a fault by saying 'that's just how I am' and resolve to treat that fault as an accident that can be removed, with Allah's help.

The Possibility Argument: Connective vs. Predicative Existence
Second proof for distinctness using possibility and the types of existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the distinctness of an accident's self-existence from its inherence, based on the difference between possibilities of self-subsistence and subsistence in another.

Core Idea
The possibility of existing by itself differs from the possibility of existing in another.

The author provides a second, independent proof that the existence of an accident in itself is distinct from its existence in the subject. He argues that the possibility of something's subsistence in itself (its 'self') is different from the possibility of its subsistence in another. This is because the first applies to conceptual matters like blindness, which subsist by their loci, while the second applies to self-subsistent essences. Thus, the two existences are different. The lesson also addresses an objection that this does not apply to accidents and clarifies that this proof is the real one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of possibility
The author introduces two types of possibility: possibility of subsistence in itself and possibility of subsistence in another. Read the passage on 'لا يخفى أن إمكان ثبوت شيء في نفسه غير إمكان ثبوته لغيره'.
This is a fundamental distinction in kalam.
2
Relate to connective vs. predicative existence
The author correlates these possibilities with connective existence (al-wujud al-ribati) and predicative existence (al-wujud al-mahmuli). The former applies to accidents like blindness, the latter to substances.
This shows that the two existences have different modes.
3
Address the objection and understand the conclusion
An objector says that this distinction does not apply to the accident because its possibility of self-subsistence is the same as its possibility of being in a subject. The author's response is that the misunderstanding claims identity of existences, not possibilities. The author concludes that this second proof is the true proof of distinctness.
The first argument only showed conceptual priority; this shows real distinctness.

The first observatory - the first aim: Definition of accident. Or reality and metaphor (with various meanings) such as the existence of a part in the whole and the universal in the particular, and like the existence of body in place or time, and like a thing being in health or illness and its being in happiness (that its existence is its existence in the subject such that they are not distinguished in sensory indication, as passed in the interpretation of indwelling). And one might misunderstand from this expression that the existence of blackness in itself, for example, is its existence in the body and its inherence in it, and it is nothing; for it is correct to say: it existed in itself then it inhered in the body. And it is not hidden that the possibility of something's subsistence in itself is other than the possibility of its subsistence in another. And they defined substance as an essence which, when it exists in the external world, is not in a subject, even if it may be in [a place]. His saying: (And like a thing being in [health]) i.e. its being in one of its states. His saying: (they are not distinguished) i.e. in actuality or in estimation, and the verification of that is that the contact of an existent with an existent completely, not by way of touch or adjacency but such that there is no separation in position between them, and a quality from the first occurs to the second, like the contact of blackness with body, is called indwelling, and the first existent is called 'indweller' and the second 'locus' — thus in Sharh al-Maqasid. His saying: (And one might misunderstand, etc.) is a refutation of what is in Sharh al-'Aqa'id of al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani where he said: The meaning of the existence of an accident in the subject is that its existence in itself is its existence in the subject, and therefore it cannot be transferred. But it agrees with what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawwani transmitted in his glosses from the comments of the Shaykh: that the existence of accidents in themselves is their existences in their subjects. His saying: (For it is correct to say, etc.) — so inherence is posterior in essence to its existence in itself. And in it [i.e., this argument] we do not concede the correctness of this statement; how [can it be] when you have said that the subject is a condition for the existence of the accident? For if existence preceded inherence, the subject would not be needed. And even if conceded, it would suffice for the ordering by the letter 'fa'' [indicating immediate sequence] the conceptual distinctness, as in their saying: 'He shot him and killed him.' His saying: (And it is not hidden that possibility, etc.) is a second proof of distinctness, and its summary is that the possibility of connective existence is different from the possibility of predicative existence, since the first is realized in conceptual matters subsisting by their loci, like blindness, and the second in essences subsisting by themselves; thus the two existences are also different. And in it [i.e., this argument]: the distinctness between the two possibilities is denied in the case of the accident, and its affirmation elsewhere is of no benefit, for the one misunderstanding says that its existence in itself is its existence in the subject. His saying: (And even if it is permissible, etc.) means that the negation of being-in-a-subject is more general than that it should not be in a locus — that is, not its existence mentioned previously, because the pronoun in 'its existence' refers to accidentalness, and the various meanings are not all like the existence of accident in the locus, as is not hidden. His saying: (For it is correct to say, etc.) This does not benefit the real distinctness which is sought; rather what benefits it is his saying: 'And it is not hidden, etc.' — so it is an independent proof for the sought.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of 'arad (accident). It begins by presenting the matn definition: 'arad is that whose existence in itself is identical to its existence in the subject, such that they are not distinguishable in sensory indication. The commentary then lists various meanings of 'existence' (e.g., part in whole, universal in particular, body in place, thing in a state like health). It then raises a misunderstanding: someone might think that the existence of blackness in itself is exactly its existence in the body and its inherence, which would make inherence unnecessary. The author refutes this by noting that we can say 'it existed in itself, then it inhered in the body,' indicating priority. Moreover, the possibility of something's subsistence in itself is different from its possibility in another. The commentary then explains the phrase 'they are not distinguished' in terms of actual or estimative non-distinction, and defines 'indwelling' (hulul) as the contact of an existent with another such that a quality accrues. It then addresses the objection from al-Taftazani's Sharh al-'Aqa'id, who held that the existence of an accident in the subject is precisely its existence in itself. The author responds that this would make the subject unnecessary, and that inherence is posterior. He also provides a second proof based on the difference between 'connective existence' (al-wujud al-ribati) and 'predicative existence' (al-wujud al-mahmuli), and their corresponding possibilities. Finally, he clarifies that the negation of being in a subject does not entail the absence of any locus, and that the various meanings of 'existence' are not all like the existence in a locus. The passage concludes by noting that the phrase 'for it is correct to say' does not prove real distinctness; rather the independent proof is the argument from possibility.

Misconception: The distinction between possibilities of self-subsistence and subsistence in another does not apply to accidents, so they could still be identical.
Correction: The author argues that the two possibilities are indeed different for accidents, as shown by the difference between connective and predicative existence.

Definition of Substance and Divisions of Accident
Defines substance and accident, enumerates divisions of accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of substance and accident according to the theologians and the main divisions of accident based on their inherence.

Core Idea
Substance is an essence with added existence; accidents either belong to living beings or are forms in matter.

The text opens by defining substance as an essence that, when it exists, is not in a subject. It stresses that existence is additional to essence for both substance and accident. The author then lists the divisions of accident, setting the foundation for kalam ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of substance
Study the first sentence carefully: substance is defined as an essence (māhiyya) that, when it exists, is not in a subject. Note that existence is additional to essence.
This is the standard Ash'ari definition.
2
Identify the divisions of accident
The passage presents two main divisions: accidents specific to living beings (like life, perceptions, knowledge, power, will) and accidents that are in a locus but not constitutive (like form in matter). List each category.
The example 'like knowledge' is given for non-sensory perceptions.
3
Understand the principle of added existence
The author says that the phrase 'when it exists' indicates that existence in the external world is additional to the essence. This is a key premise for the later discussion about God.
This principle is fundamental in distinguishing creatures from the Creator.

The first observatory - the second aim: The accident and its divisions according to the theologians. A locus like the bodily form inhering in matter, and they indicated by their saying 'when it exists' that existence is additional to the essence in substance and accident, and from that the definition of substance does not apply to the Essence of the Almighty. [The second aim: The accident and its divisions according to the theologians] (Concerning its divisions according to the theologians, and it is the accident) either it is specific to the living being, namely life and what follows it of perceptions by the senses, and other things like knowledge and power and will, like the separate substances, prime matter, and body, or it is in a locus but is not constitutive of it, like form in relation to matter. His saying: (And they indicated, etc.) means that their saying 'when it exists' is an indication that the existence by which it is existent in the external world is additional to the essence in substance and accident, as is the immediate understanding. His saying: (it does not apply, etc.) because His existentness is by an existence that is the very essence, even if absolute existence is additional to it, and by this is repelled what was said: The definition of substance does not require the addition of specific existence to the essence, but rather the addition of absolute existence, and the philosophers assert its addition, so the Necessary does not exit. And it may be said: The essence indicates universality by implication, so by the restriction 'essence' the Necessary exits, but it is nothing — and it is nothing — because it excludes particular substances from the definition, and because universality is necessary to essence in the sense of that by which the question 'what is it?' is answered, which is the terminology of logic, not essence in the sense of that by which a thing is itself, which is the terminology of philosophers. And it was said: Our saying 'when it exists' suggests the possibility of existence, so it does not apply to Him, may He be exalted. And in it: The suggestion of general possibility is conceded, and it is realized in the Necessary; the suggestion of specific possibility is denied. His saying: (like knowledge, etc.) is an example of other than them, based on the fact that sensory perception is not knowledge, and therefore some added a restriction between meanings in its definition as passed. His saying: (And they indicated by their saying 'when it exists', etc.) There is discussion in it because the definition of substance does not require the addition of specific existence to the essence, but rather the addition of absolute existence, and the philosophers assert the addition of absolute existence as passed in the discussion of existence, so the Necessary, may He be exalted, does not exit from the definition by His saying 'when it exists' — unless it is said: The immediate understanding when the attribution of existence to a thing is mentioned is its specific existence; and if it exits by His saying 'essence' based on considering universality in essence as he indicated at the beginning of universal matters, and that it requires the addition of specific existence, it would not be far. And it may be said: The source of the non-applicability of this definition to the Necessary, may He be exalted, is that our saying 'an essence which, when it exists, is such and such' suggests the possibility of non-existence, so it does not apply to Him, and the principle is the addition of existence, but considering such suggestion in definitions is far-fetched. His saying: (of perceptions by the senses) — he did not make his saying 'like knowledge' an example of perceptions on the way of lighaf wa'l-nashr because the well-known usage is 'sensation' for perception, and because that which is more appropriate then would be 'like sciences'.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of substance and the divisions of accident. It begins with matn defining substance as an essence that when existing externally is not in a subject, and states that existence is additional to essence in both substance and accident, so the definition of substance does not apply to God (since His existence is His essence). The commentary then addresses objections: some say the definition requires only absolute existence not specific, so God is not excluded; the response is that the definition implies general possibility (which applies to God) not specific. It then presents the divisions of accident: either specific to living beings (like life and sense-perceptions, and others like knowledge, power, will) or not specific (like separate substances, prime matter, body) or in a locus but not constitutive (like form in matter). The commentary also discusses the example 'like knowledge' and clarifies that sensory perception is not knowledge per se. Finally, it revisits the substance definition and discusses whether the phrase 'when it exists' suggests possibility and thus excludes God; it offers several responses, ultimately concluding that the intent is general possibility which applies to God.

Substance and accident are defined by the addition of existence to essence, and accidents are divided into three categories based on their relation to the living and to matter.

?

How does this change the way you view your appearance, aging, or physical abilities? What would it look like to treat your body as a contingent accident rather than the core of your identity?

Why the Definition of Substance Does Not Apply to God
Discusses why substance definition excludes God due to identity of existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments about why the definition of substance does not apply to God, focusing on the distinction between general and specific possibility and the nature of divine existence.

Core Idea
God is excluded from the definition because His existence is identical to His essence, not added.

The previous lesson introduced the definition of substance as an essence with added existence. This lesson examines objections to the claim that this definition does not apply to God. The commentary clarifies that for God, existence is not additional but identical to essence, and addresses philosophical objections by distinguishing general possibility from specific possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
The passage begins by explaining why the definition of substance does not apply to God: His existentness is by an existence that is the very essence. Then it addresses the objection that the definition only requires absolute existence, not specific existence.
The initial response uses the distinction between absolute and specific existence.
2
Analyze the second objection
Another objection says that the definition suggests possibility of non-existence, so it excludes God. The response distinguishes general possibility (which applies to God) from specific possibility (which does not).
General possibility means 'not impossible', specific means 'contingent'.
3
Understand the resolution
The passage concludes that the definition implies only general possibility, which is realized in the Necessary Existent, so it does not exclude God. Thus, the earlier claim that the definition does not apply remains valid only if specific possibility is intended.
This shows the precision of theological language.

His saying: (of perceptions by the senses) — he did not make his saying 'like knowledge' an example of perceptions on the way of lighaf wa'l-nashr because the well-known usage is 'sensation' for perception, and because that which is more appropriate then would be 'like sciences'.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of substance and the divisions of accident. It begins with matn defining substance as an essence that when existing externally is not in a subject, and states that existence is additional to essence in both substance and accident, so the definition of substance does not apply to God (since His existence is His essence). The commentary then addresses objections: some say the definition requires only absolute existence not specific, so God is not excluded; the response is that the definition implies general possibility (which applies to God) not specific. It then presents the divisions of accident: either specific to living beings (like life and sense-perceptions, and others like knowledge, power, will) or not specific (like separate substances, prime matter, body) or in a locus but not constitutive (like form in matter). The commentary also discusses the example 'like knowledge' and clarifies that sensory perception is not knowledge per se. Finally, it revisits the substance definition and discusses whether the phrase 'when it exists' suggests possibility and thus excludes God; it offers several responses, ultimately concluding that the intent is general possibility which applies to God.
