Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the definition of substance excludes God because it implies possibility of non-existence, which is false for God.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the definition only implies general possibility, which applies even to the Necessary Existent. The exclusion is due to existence being identical to essence, not possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction used to refute the objection that 'when it exists' suggests impossibility for God?
A) Between absolute and specific existence.

C) Between substance and accident.
D) Between essence and existence.

Key Takeaway
The definition of substance does not apply to God because His existence is identical to His essence, and any suggestion of possibility refers only to general possibility, not contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your existence is added to your essence—you could not exist, and you depend on God every moment. When you feel self-sufficient, are you not effectively denying that your existence is a gift? In what specific situation this week will you consciously remind yourself of your contingency before God?
?
God's existence is identical to His essence: He is existence itself. How does this truth expose your neediness? If God stopped willing your existence for a single second, you would vanish. How would your prayer change if you truly believed this?
?
The passage distinguishes between general possibility (not impossible) and specific possibility (contingency). You are contingent, not just 'not impossible'. When did you last weep in humility because your life hangs by the thread of God's will? What will you do today to cultivate that awareness?

What Is Kalam?
beginner
4 min
The Two Divisions of Accidents
Defines the two kinds of accidents: specific to living beings and universal (states and sense-perceptibles).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main divisions of accidents according to the mutakallimun: those specific to living beings and those that are not, including the akwan (states) and sensible qualities.

Core Idea
Accidents are either tied to life or universal to all bodies.

This lesson introduces the foundational classification of accidents (al-a'rad) in Islamic theology. The mutakallimun divide accidents into two broad categories: those that are specific to living beings and those that are not. The latter includes the four states (akwan) of motion, rest, union, and separation, as well as sense-perceptible qualities like sounds and colors.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Read the Arabic passage once to see the two categories: specific to living and not specific.
2
Identify examples
Note the examples for each category: desire, appetite, aversion for specific; motion, rest, union, separation, and sensible things for universal.
3
Reflect on importance
Consider why this classification is a building block for further theological arguments about attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثاني : العرض وأقسامه عند المتكلمين والكراهة والشهوة والنفرة وسائر ما يتبع الحياة وحصرها في عشرة باطل بلا شبهة ( وإما أن لا يختص به وهو الأكوان ( المنحصرة في أنواع أربعة : الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق، ( والمحسوسات ) بإحدى الحواس الخمس كالأصوات والألوان والروائح والطعوم والحرارة وأخواتها . قوله : ( وحصرها إلخ) كما حصرها صاحب الصحائف في عشرة الحياة والقدرة والاعتقاد والظن وكلام النفس والإرادة والكراهة والشهوة والنفرة والألم كذا نقله بعض الناظرين وبطلانه أظهر من أن يخفى على من له أدنى فطانة ولعمري كيف خفي على ذلك الفاضل .
The First Observatory - The Second Objective: The Accident and Its Divisions According to the Mutakallimun. And desire, appetite, aversion, and all that follows life, and its enumeration as ten is false without doubt. ( And that which is not specific to it, which is the akwan (states) (encompassed in four types: motion, rest, union, and separation. ( And the sensible things ) by one of the five senses, like sounds, colors, smells, tastes, heat, and their sisters. His saying: (And its enumeration etc.) as the author of al-Saha'if enumerated it as ten: life, power, belief, opinion, self-speech, will, desire, appetite, aversion, and pain. Thus some scholars transmitted it, and its falsity is clearer than to be hidden from anyone with the least intelligence. By my life, how was that hidden from that virtuous one!
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a definition and a critique. It first defines the two main divisions of accident (al-'arad) according to the mutakallimun: (1) accidents specific to living beings (al-mukhtassa bi al-hayy), which include desire, appetite, aversion, and everything else that follows life, and (2) accidents not specific to living beings (al-ghayr al-mukhtassa), which are the four akwan (states) of motion, rest, union, and separation. It then mentions sensible accidents (al-mahsusat) perceived by the five senses. The passage also includes a critique of a particular enumeration that limits the specific accidents to ten types, as done by the author of al-Saha'if. The commentary explains that this tenfold enumeration is false because it excludes other evident specific accidents like astonishment, laughter, joy, grief, etc. The passage emphasizes the baselessness of that enumeration.

Key Terms
عرض (alarad) accident
مختص بالحي (mukhtass bil-hayy) specific to living beings
أكوان (akwan) states (motion, rest, union, separation)
محسوسات (mahsusat) sensible things

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT part of the accidents specific to living beings?
A) desire
B) appetite
C) motion
D) aversion

Key Takeaway
Accidents are divided into those specific to living beings and those universal to all bodies, the latter including states and sense-perceptibles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your desires and aversions are mere accidents—temporary, not essential. Do you live as if they define your identity, or do you recognize their fleeting nature and seek what is permanent? Today, consciously detach from one passing emotion and submit it to your Lord.
?
The sensibles you perceive—colors, sounds, tastes—are accidents dependent on created bodies. Do you treat them as ultimate realities, forgetting they are signs pointing to the Creator? Commit to viewing one sensory pleasure today as a reminder of Allah, not an end in itself.
?
The mutakallimun assert that enumerating Allah's creation in a fixed number is false. Where in your life have you limited Allah's power by confining His attributes or actions to your own categories? Repent by acknowledging that He creates beyond your understanding.

The Fallacy of the Tenfold Enumeration
Criticizes the tenfold enumeration of specific accidents as false and baseless.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the tenfold enumeration of specific accidents by the author of al-Saha'if is considered false by the mutakallimun.

Core Idea
Limiting specific accidents to ten is baseless; many more are evident.

This lesson focuses on the critique of a particular enumeration that claims accidents specific to living beings are only ten. The passage condemns this enumeration as palpably false and wonders how a learned scholar could have been misled. We will see the list and understand why it is incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
Read the passage that quotes the tenfold list and the strong condemnation.
2
Examine the list
Identify the ten items enumerated: life, power, belief, opinion, self-speech, will, desire, appetite, aversion, pain.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Consider why the author says the falsity is obvious: many other specific accidents like astonishment, laughter, etc. are missing.

?

?

What Is Kalam?

Classification of Accidents and Definition of Quantity
Introduces the division of accidents into nine categories and defines quantity as divisible per se.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosophers classify accidents into nine categories and define quantity as that which accepts division per se, including the distinction between continuous and discrete quantity and the two meanings of division.

Core Idea
The classification of all accidents depends on the notion of divisibility, which uniquely defines quantity.

In this passage, we enter the discussion of accidents according to the philosophers. They held that all accidents are restricted to nine categories, and all substances form a single category, making ten highest genera for possible existents. Their enumeration is based on incomplete induction, but they provide a systematic key: the accident either accepts division per se or not. The one that does is quantity. This simple criterion allows them to define quantity and explore its properties, including the crucial distinction between continuous and discrete quantity, and the two different senses in which a thing can be said to be divisible. The passage ends with a warning: one cannot define quantity simply by 'acceptance of division' because that applies only to continuous quantity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the classification into nine categories
Note that the philosophers claim accidents are confined to nine categories, while substances are one category, making ten total. This is based on incomplete induction, and they provide a method to reduce the spread.
The method is to ask whether the accident accepts division per se.
2
Define quantity as divisible per se
Quantity is defined as that which accepts division in its essence, not accidentally. An example of accidental divisibility is knowledge of two things, which is divisible only because of its objects.
'Per se' means without mediation of another thing.
3
Distinguish the two meanings of division
The passage presents two meanings from al-Mulakhkhaṣ: (1) conceptual division – assuming one part different from another within the same thing, and (2) actual separation – resulting in two distinct identities. The first applies to magnitude, the second to matter.
Understanding this distinction is key to avoiding errors.
4
Recognize why defining quantity by division is problematic
The text concludes that defining quantity by acceptance of division is incorrect because it only applies to continuous quantity, not discrete (like number). The correct definition involves equality and inequality.
This is a major point of contention among philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : العرض وأقسامه عند الحكماء ]
في أقسامه عند الحكماء ذهب الحكماء إلى أنه أي العرض ( منحصر في المقولات التسع وأنَّ الجواهر كلها مقولة واحدة فصارت المقولات التي هي أجناس عالية للموجودات الممكنة عشراً ) ولم يأتوا في الحصر بما يصلح للاعتماد عليه وعمدتهم) في إثبات الحصر هو ( الاستقراء ) الناقص ووجه ضبطه بحيث يقلل من الانتشار ويسهل الاستقراء أنهم ( قالوا : العرض إما أن يقبل لذاته القسمة أم لا والأول ) هو ( الكم وإنما قلنا لذاته ليخرج عن الحد ( الكم بالعرض كالعلم بمعلومين ) فإنه قابل للقسمة لكن لا لذاته بل لتعلقه بالمعلومين المعروضين للعدد وسيرد عليك أقسام الكم بالعرض ( والمراد بالقسمة هنا يعني في حد الكم ( أن يفرض فيه شيء غير شيء فيدخل في المتصل والمنفصل ( لأن كلا منهما قابل للقسمة بهذا المعنى وذكر في الملخص أن قبول القسمة قد يراد به كون الشيء بحيث يمكن أن يُفرض فيه شيء غير شيء وهذا المعنى يلحق المقدار لذاته وقد يراد به الافتراق بحيث يحدث للجسم هويتان وهذا المعنى لا يلحق المقدار لأن الملحوق يجب بقاؤه عند اللاحق والمقدار الواحد إذا انفصل فقد عدم وحصل هناك مقداران لم يكونا موجودين بالفعل قبل الانفصال بل القابل للانقسام بهذا المعنى هو المادة والمقدار في قبولها إياه ثم ذكر فيه أنه لا يجوز تعريف الكم بقبول القسمة لأنه مختص بالمتصل

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : العرض وأقسامه عند الحكماء
قوله : ( إما أن يقبل لذاته (القسمة أي يكون معروضاً لها بلا واسطة أمر آخر . قوله : ( أقسام الكم بالعرض وهو محل الكم بالذات أو الحال فيه أو الحال في محله أو متعلقه .
قوله : (لأن كلاً منهما إلخ) وكون الأجزاء حاصلة بالفعل لا ينافي فرضها بل هو أعون على الفرض .
قوله : ( يحدث للجسم خص الجسم بالذكر إشارة إلى أن هذه القسمة تلحق الجسم لذاته لأنه لا بد فيه من الحركة وإنما تلحق الأعراض بالتبع.

The Third Objective: The Accident and Its Divisions according to the Philosophers. [Commentary:] On its divisions according to the philosophers: the philosophers held that the accident (al-ʿaraḍ) is confined to the nine categories, and that all substances (jawāhir) are a single category; thus the categories that are high genera for possible existents become ten. And they did not bring in the enumeration anything reliable; their basis in proving the enumeration is incomplete induction (istiqrāʾ nāqiṣ). The way to control it so as to reduce spread and facilitate induction is that they said: The accident either accepts division (qisma) per se or not. The first is quantity (al-kamm). We said 'per se' to exclude quantity by accident, like knowledge of two things, for it is divisible but not per se, rather due to its relation to the two things that are subjects of number. The divisions of quantity by accident will come to you. What is meant by division here, in the definition of quantity, is that something other than something be assumed in it, so it includes the continuous and the discrete, because each of them is divisible in this sense. It was mentioned in al-Mulakhkhaṣ that acceptance of division may mean that the thing is such that something other than something can be assumed in it—this meaning attaches to magnitude (al-miqdār) per se—and it may mean separation such that two identities come to be for the body—this meaning does not attach to magnitude, because what is followed must persist when the follower comes, and a single magnitude when separated ceases to exist and two magnitudes come to be that did not actually exist before separation; rather that which is divisible in this sense is matter (al-mādda), and magnitude in its acceptance of it. Then it was mentioned there that it is not permissible to define quantity by the acceptance of division because it is specific to the continuous. The First Observatory – The Third Objective: The Accident and Its Divisions according to the Philosophers. His statement: (either accepts division per se) means it is a subject for it without the mediation of another thing. His statement: (the divisions of quantity by accident) – it is the place of quantity per se or that which is in it or that which is in its place or its relation. His statement: (because each of them etc.) – and that the parts are actually present does not contradict assuming them; rather it helps the assumption. His statement: (occurs for the body) – he specified the body to indicate that this division attaches to the body per se because it necessarily involves motion, and it attaches to accidents only by way of followership. His statement: (it is not permissible to define quantity etc.) – in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya, some restricted the definition of quantity to the acceptance of equality and inequality, and some added to it the acceptance of division, and that is an error, because the acceptance of division is an accident of continuous quantity, not of discrete quantity, unless acceptance is taken by homonymy. The end. What is understood from it is that it is not permissible to define it by acceptance of division absolutely, and that the source of the error is the word 'acceptance'. Its explanation: if by it is meant assuming and being characterized, and by division is meant assumed division, since separation division only occurs to matter, then it is specific to the continuous without the discrete, either because 'assumed' is only applied to that which is opposite of intellectual, as they stated in the definition of the part, or because of the consideration of the condition of non-discontinuity in it; and if by it is meant occurrence and by division separation division, then it is specific to the continuous because... His statement: (and facilitates induction) – it was said: the way of facilitating induction is that what is meant is only the last division; thus induction is specific to it, and one investigates whether multiple instances of it exist or not; and there is no need for induction in the remaining judgments because they are obtained by mental division (tardīd ʿaqlī).
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is a combination of the main text (matn) of the Third Objective on accidents and its commentary. It begins by stating that the philosophers (al-ḥukamāʾ) hold that accidents are confined to nine categories, and that all substances are a single category, making ten highest genera for possible existents. The enumeration is based on incomplete induction, and the commentators explain how to make the induction easier. The main text then defines quantity (al-kamm) as that which accepts division per se, excluding quantity by accident (like knowledge of two things, which is divisible only due to its objects). Division (qisma) is defined as the assumption of something other than something within the quantity, which applies to both continuous (al-muttaṣil) and discrete (al-munfaṣil) quantity. The commentator clarifies two meanings of division: (1) the conceptual assumption of parts, which applies to magnitude per se, and (2) actual separation, which does not apply to magnitude because when a magnitude separates, it ceases to exist and two new magnitudes come to be. The latter meaning applies to matter. The commentator then discusses a controversy: some define quantity by acceptance of division, but this is rejected because divisibility is a property of continuous quantity only, not discrete quantity, unless the word 'acceptance' is used homonymously. The correct definition is acceptance of equality and inequality. Finally, there is a marginal note about induction: the induction is facilitated by focusing only on the last division (probably discrete quantity or something similar), so that one only needs to check if there are multiple instances of that division, as the other divisions are obtained by mental division (tardīd ʿaqlī).

Correction: Division only applies to continuous quantity, not discrete. The correct definition involves equality and inequality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers relied on incomplete induction to classify accidents. In your own search for truth about Allah and His attributes, are you relying on incomplete evidence or comfortable assumptions? What is one area where your certainty exceeds your evidence, and how will you rectify that?
?
The passage distinguishes between per se and accidental divisibility: some things are divisible by their own nature, others only through relation. Examine your own character: which of your traits are essential to who you are, and which are merely accidental? How does confusing the two lead you away from Allah's purpose for you?
?
Acceptance of division here means a mental assumption, not a physical act. In your spiritual life, there is a difference between knowing a truth and living it out. Where have you merely assumed the parts of faith in your mind without allowing them to actually separate you from sin? Commit to one concrete change today.

Definition of Kayf (Quality) and the Objection of Unity
Defines quality and refutes the objection that unity is a quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of quality (kayf) as a non-relative, indivisible accident and why unity (waḥda) does not fall under this definition.

Core Idea
Quality is a non-relative, indivisible accident; unity is privative, not an existent accident.

In this passage from Sharḥ al-Mawāqif, the author defines the category of quality (kayf) by distinguishing it from relative accidents. He then addresses a potential counterexample: unity (waḥda) appears to meet the definition but is excluded because it is not an existent accident. This lesson unpacks that definition and the defense against the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get a feel for the argument's flow.
The Arabic contains some unclear phrases; focus on the main ideas.
2
Identify the definition of quality
Find the sentence that defines quality. Note that the author deliberately uses 'rasm' (definition by description) because the highest genera are simple.
The definition is: 'an accident that does not accept division per se and does not entail relation per se.'
3
Understand the objection and its answer
The objection is that unity (waḥda) fits this definition: it is indivisible and non-relative. Find the answer given in the passage.
The answer: unity is privative (ʿadamiyya), so it is not an accident (which is a division of existence).
4
Examine the deeper discussion on unity's existence
The commentary reveals a debate among philosophers: some hold unity to be privative, others consider it existent. See how the author reconciles these views.
This part is more advanced; focus on the main point that the objection fails.

The second (i.e., that which does not accept division per se) is either such that its concept entails relation per se, i.e., its meaning is understood in relation to another, or does not entail relation. The second is al-kayf (quality), and its definition (rasm) is given — he explicitly says 'definition (rasm)' to indicate that the highest genera are simple and cannot have a real definition (ḥadd ḥaqīqī), as will be clarified —: 'an accident that does not accept division per se and does not entail relation per se.' This definition will be disclosed in the third observatory. Thus, the objection regarding unity (al-waḥda) does not apply to the definition of quality, because unity is privative (ʿadamiyya) and thus does not fall under accident, which is a division of existence. His statement: (i.e., its concept being understood in relation to another) means that the intended 'entailment' is not entailment of relation in the external world, for then knowledge would enter [the definition] since it entails relation to the known externally while being a quality. Rather, the meaning of 'being understood in relation to another' is that its meaning is not established in the mind except by noting another, i.e., something external to its bearer — not that it depends on it. Thus, relation (al-iḍāfa) [as a category] is excluded, whether its concept is relation itself like iḍāfa or something subject to relation like waḍʿ and milk. His statement: (or does not entail relation) [means] the extent of the relation's connection by the preceding indication, for it is not intended that it does not accept [?...]. His statement: (because it is privative, etc.) This answer is based on the doctrine of the verifying philosophers that unity is privative, and likewise number, and it is counted under quantity (al-kamm) by way of treating it as existent because the principle of its abstraction exists, just as they said about the existence of motion in the sense of traversal and time in the sense of extension due to the existence of their principles. As for those who affirm their existence, they add the condition of indivisibility to its definition, as will come. His statement: (because it is privative) there is a discussion, because our discourse is according to the doctrine of the philosophers, and unity is certainly existent in their view, otherwise discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfaṣil), i.e., number which has no part besides units, would not exist. Know that the commentator of al-Maqāṣid mentioned in the discussions of quantity that the philosophers do not consider number among external existents but rather among mental considerations, and that the disagreement of the theologians with them is due to their denial of mental existence. By this it is imagined that the discussion is refuted, but their proof for the existence of number indicates their claim of external existence, as you will see from what is coming, and his statement indicates that they consider number — which is the sum of units — to be among accidents. It will become clear. And that they considered it to have external existence. So reconciling these statements with the judgment of the privativity of unity is a matter for tears, and there will be a completion of this discussion, God willing.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage defines the category of quality (kayf) and defends it against an objection. It begins by classifying accidents into two types: those whose concept entails relation to another (these are relations, i.e., the nine relative categories) and those that do not. The second type is quality. The author deliberately uses the term 'rasm' (definition by description) because the highest genera are simple and cannot have a real definition (ḥadd). The definition given is: an accident that is not divisible per se and does not entail relation per se. This definition is said to be clarified later. An objection arises: unity (waḥda) seems to fit this definition — it is not divisible and does not entail relation — yet it is not considered a quality. The answer is that unity is privative (ʿadamiyya), not an existent accident, since accidents are divisions of existence. The commentary elaborates: the answer is based on the doctrine of the verifying philosophers (muḥaqqiqūn) who hold that unity and number are privative but are counted under quantity because their abstraction originates from existent things. Those who affirm the existence of unity add the condition of indivisibility to the definition of quality to exclude it. A further discussion follows: if unity is privative, why do philosophers treat it as existent? The commentator notes the view from Sharḥ al-Maqāṣid that numbers are mental considerations, not external, reconciling the two positions by suggesting that the philosophers' proofs for the existence of number refer to mental existence, not external. This reconciliation is difficult.

الكيف (kayf)
الوحدة (waḥda)
العدم (ʿadam)
الرسم (rasm)
المحققون (muḥaqqiqūn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Unity (waḥda) is a quality because it is a non-relative, indivisible accident.
Correction: Unity is privative (non-existent), and accidents are divisions of existence, so unity cannot be a quality.

In your understanding of Allah, are you content with descriptive knowledge, or do you demand definitive definitions that may be impossible? The Nine Accident Categories via Nisba
Enumeration of accident categories via types of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how philosophers divide accidents into nine categories by analyzing the types of relation (nisba) they involve.

Core Idea
Every accident relates either to its subject's parts or to something external.

In this passage, the author explains a key philosophical classification: accidents are not random but fall into nine distinct categories. The key is understanding that every accident involves a relation (nisba) – either between parts of its subject or between the subject and something external. By examining the possible objects of that relation, we arrive at nine accident categories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of nisba
Read the passage with the idea that every accident is defined by a relation. Note the two primary types: relation between parts of the subject (position) or relation of the whole subject to something external.
2
Follow the classification tree
Trace how the external relation splits based on what it relates to: quantity (stable or non-stable), relation itself, quality, or substance.
3
Handle the special case of substance
Notice that relation to substance is not an independent category; it reduces to one of the previous three. This maintains nine accident categories.
4
Confirm the final count
Verify that substance plus nine accidents equals ten total categories of existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : العرض وأقسامه عند الحكماء
أي لأجزاء موضوعها بعضها إلى بعض ( وهو الوضع أو لا تكون النسبة بين أجزاء موضوعها بل لمجموعه إلى أمر خارج عنه ( وهي ( أي هذه النسبة (إما إلى كم فإن ) كان ) ذلك الكم ( قاراً) لجواز اجتماع أجزائه معا ( فإن انتقل) ذلك الكم القار ( به ) أي بانتقال موضوع النسبة ) فهو الملك وإلا فهو الأين وإن كان ذلك الكم ( غير قار فهو متى وإما إلى النسبة فالمضاف ) لأن النسبة حينئذ متكررة ( وإما إلى كيف ولا ) ) تعقل النسبة إلى الكيف إلا) بأن يكون منه غيره وهو أن يفعل أو ) يكون هو من غيره وهو أن ينفعل وإما إلى الجوهر وهو لا يقبل النسبة لذاته بل لعارض من عوارضه ( ولا يخرجُ ) ذلك العارض ( مما ذكرنا ) من الأعراض الثلاثة فالبنسبة إلى الجوهر تكون راجعة إلى النسب المذكورة لا قسما برأسه فانحصرت الممكنات الموجودة في عشر مقولات والأعراض في تسع منها
The First Observatory - The Third Intent: The Accident and Its Divisions according to the Philosophers. That is, for the parts of its subject to one another (which is position), or the relation is not between the parts of its subject but rather of its whole to something external to it. And it (i.e., this relation) is either to quantity, and if that quantity is stable because its parts can exist together, then if that stable quantity is transferred by the transfer of the subject of the relation, it is possession; otherwise it is location. And if that quantity is not stable, it is time. And either to relation, which is the relative, because the relation then is repeated. And either to quality, and the relation to quality is only conceivable by it being from another (i.e., it produces another) which is to act, or it being from another (i.e., it is produced by another) which is to be acted upon. And either to substance, which does not receive relation in itself but only through an accident from its accidents, and that accident does not depart from what we have mentioned of the three accidents. So in relation to substance, it reverts to the mentioned relations, not an independent category. Thus the possible existents are limited to ten categories, and the accidents to nine of them.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage defines the division of accidents into nine categories based on the type of relation (nisba) an accident has. It begins by explaining that relation can be either between parts of the subject (called 'position') or between the whole subject and something external. Each such relation is then classified: to quantity (stable or non-stable, giving rise to possession, location, and time), to relation itself (giving the relative), to quality (as action or passion), and to substance. However, substance does not receive relation in itself but only through an accident, and that accident reduces to one of the previous three. Therefore, the possible accidents are enumerated from these relations, resulting in nine categories (since substance itself is one category, making ten total). The passage follows the order of the matn, with explanatory insertions clarifying each term.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The relation to substance is an independent accident category.
Correction: The passage states that substance does not receive relation in itself; any relation to substance is via an accident that belongs to one of the previous three categories (quantity, relation, or quality), so it is not independent.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you content with knowing Allah through His attributes, or do you seek to understand His essence beyond them? How does this lesson humble you in your pursuit of knowledge of Allah?

The Limitation of Categories and the Weakness of Induction
Al-Isfahani states the inductive basis of the categories and presents alternative classifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the philosophical classification of all possible things into ten categories is based on induction, which yields only weak opinion, and that this led to alternative schemes.

Core Idea
The ten categories are not certain; they rely on weak induction.

Al-Isfahani begins by stating a sobering fact: the famous ten categories are not proven demonstratively. The philosophers themselves admit that their only method is induction, which gives only probable opinion. As a result, some scholars proposed different numbers, reducing to four or adding motion as a fifth.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the phrase 'min al-mashhurat' and 'la yufid illa zannan da'ifan'.
This immediately establishes the epistemological status.
2
Identify the key claim
The author says the limitation is based on induction (istiqra'), which yields only weak opinion. Understand that this is a critique of certainty.
3
Note the alternative classifications
Because of the weakness, some scholars proposed four categories (substance, quantity, quality, relation) and others five (adding motion). Reflect on what this implies.
The existence of alternatives underscores the non-demonstrative nature.

اعلم أنَّ انحصار الممكنات في هذه المقولات من المشهورات فيما بينهم وهم معترفون بأنه لا سبيل لهم إليه سوي الاستقراء الذي لا يفيد إلا ظناً ضعيفاً ولذلك خالفه بعضهم فجعل المقولات أربعاً الجوهر والكم والكيف والنسبة الشاملة للسبعة الباقية وبعضهم جعلها خمساً فعد الحركة مقولة برأسها وقد صرحوا بأن المقولات أجناس عالية للموجودات وأن المفهومات الاعتبارية من الأمور العامة وغيرها سواء كانت ثبوتية أو عدمية كالوجود والشيئية والإمكان والعمى والجهل ليست مندرجة فيها وكذلك مفهومات المشتقات نحو الأبيض والأسود خارجة عنها لأنها أجناس لماهيات لها وحدة نوعية مثل السواد والبياض والإنسان والفرس وكون الشيء ذا بياض لا يتحصل به ماهية نوعية. قوله : (لا يفيد إلا ظناً ضعيفاً الاستقراء الناقص إنما يفيد الظن قبل الفوز بقسم آخر وأما إذا وجد قسم آخر كما فيما نحن فيه فلا يفيده أصلاً اللهم إلا أن يقام الحجة على انتفاء ما مر من الأقسام ويمكن أن يكون مراده سوى الاستقراء الذي لا يفيد بحسب نوعه إلا ظناً ضعيفاً وهو الاستقراء الناقص فجملة الموصول مع الصلة صفة للاستقراء قائم مقام الناقص فتأمل . قوله : ( والنسبة الشاملة للسبعة الباقية ) فالنسبة على هذا جنس للسبعة وأما على تقدير انحصار المقولات في التسع فهي عرض عام للمقولات السبع.
Know that the limitation of possible things to these categories is among the well-known (mashhurat) among them, and they acknowledge that they have no path to it except induction (istiqra'), which yields only weak opinion (zann). Therefore some opposed them, making the categories four: substance (jawhar), quantity (kamm), quality (kayf), and relation (nisba) encompassing the remaining seven. And some made them five, counting motion (haraka) as a separate category. And they explicitly stated that the categories are highest genera (ajnas 'aliya) for existents, and that conceptual notions (al-mafahim al-i'tibariyya) from universal matters and others, whether affirmative or negative like existence (wujud), thingness (shay'iyya), possibility (imkan), blindness ('ama), and ignorance (jahl), are not included in them. Likewise, the concepts of derived terms (mushtaqqat) like white (abyad) and black (aswad) are outside them, because they are genera for essences (mahiyyat) having specific unity (wahda naw'iyya) like blackness (sawad), whiteness (bayad), man (insan), and horse (faras), and a thing's being possessed of whiteness does not yield a specific essence. His statement: 'which yields only weak opinion' — incomplete induction yields opinion only before coming across another division; but if another division is found, as in our case, it yields nothing at all, unless a proof is established for the negation of the other divisions. It is possible that his meaning is 'other than induction that by its type yields only weak opinion', i.e., incomplete induction, so the relative clause is an adjective for induction standing in place of 'incomplete', so reflect. His statement: 'and the relation encompassing the remaining seven' — relation on this view is a genus for the seven; but on the assumption of the limitation of categories to nine, it is a common accident ('arad 'amm) for the seven categories.
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the classification of categories (maqulat) and the epistemological status of this classification. It begins by stating that the limitation of all possible existing things to these ten categories is a matter of common acceptance (mashhurat) among the philosophers, not demonstrative certainty. They admit that their only method for establishing this limitation is induction (al-istiqra'), which yields only weak opinion (zann da'if). Because of this weakness, some scholars dissented: one group reduced the categories to four (substance, quantity, quality, and relation encompassing the other seven), and another group made them five by counting motion as a separate category. Al-Isfahani then notes that the philosophers explicitly state that these categories are the highest genera (ajnas 'aliya) of existent things. Following this, he clarifies what is excluded from the categories: conceptual, mental considerations (al-mafahim al-i'tibariyya) whether positive (like existence, thingness, possibility) or negative (like blindness, ignorance), as well as the concepts of derived terms (like 'white' and 'black') because these are genera for specific essences such as blackness and whiteness, and a thing's being white does not give it a specific essence. The commentary then explains the remark about weak opinion: incomplete induction only yields opinion before encountering another case; if another case is encountered, as here (where alternative divisions are proposed), it yields nothing unless a proof is given that those divisions are impossible. The commentator also suggests that the author might have meant 'the incomplete induction that by its nature yields only weak opinion', so the relative clause serves as an adjective for 'incomplete'. Finally, the commentary explains the phrase 'relation encompassing the remaining seven': on the view of four categories, relation is a genus for the seven subordinate categories; on the standard view of nine categories (excluding relation as a common accident), relation is a common accident (al-'arad al-'amm) for those seven.

استقراء
مشهورات
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ten categories are demonstratively proven.
Correction: The passage explicitly states they are based on induction, which yields only weak opinion, and alternatives exist.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the epistemological status of the classification of all possible things into the ten categories?
A) It is known by necessity (daruri).
B) It is based on scriptural evidence (naqli).
C) It is based on induction (istiqra'), which yields only weak opinion.
D) It is a direct intuition (hadsi).

Key Takeaway
The ten categories are a product of induction, not demonstration, and therefore admit of alternative classifications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly about the world. Have you considered whether it is based on common acceptance (mashhurat) or weak induction (zann da'if)? Will you examine its foundation today, or will you keep assuming without evidence?
?
The philosophers admitted the weakness of their own methodology. Are you as honest about the limits of your reasoning? List one area where you might be overconfident—can you commit to seeking certainty rather than comfortable assumptions?
?
If a widely accepted classification of existence is open to alternative schemes, what does that imply for your own certainties about metaphysics? Will you rely more on revelation and clear demonstration, or will you cling to weak inductions? Decide now: what step will you take to ground your convictions more firmly?

What the Categories Are Not: Exclusions and Clarifications
Al-Isfahani clarifies the scope of categories and explains the limitations of induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that conceptual notions and derived terms are not included among the categories, and you will grasp the refined understanding of induction and the status of relation.

Core Idea
Categories are only for specific essences; conceptual notions and derived attributes are excluded.

After presenting the categories themselves, al-Isfahani clarifies their scope. Not everything that can be conceptually distinguished is a category. He explains that conceptual mental considerations, whether positive or negative, and concepts like 'white' derived from whiteness, are not categories because they do not constitute a specific essence. He also comments on the nature of induction and the status of relation.

Guided Path
1
Read the exclusions
Read the part about conceptual notions (mafahim i'tibariyya) and derived terms (mushtaqqat). Notice the examples given.
The passage lists both affirmative and negative concepts.
2
Understand why conceptual notions are excluded
Concepts like existence and blindness are mental considerations that do not form a specific essence with specific unity. They are not highest genera.
3
Understand why derived terms are excluded
Terms like 'white' refer to an attribute, not the essence of whiteness itself. They are derived from categories, not categories themselves.
4
Examine the commentary on induction and relation
The passage explains that incomplete induction loses force when counterexamples appear, and that relation can be a genus or a common accident depending on the classification scheme.
This refines the earlier discussion.

?

Classification of Accidents: Motion vs. Stationary
Al-Isfahani classifies accidents into stationary and non-stationary, and subdivides stationary accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how accidents are classified into stationary and non-stationary, and how motion fits as a non-stationary accident.

Core Idea
Motion is the non-stationary accident; all other accidents are stationary.

Before delving into the specifics of motion, al-Isfahani first establishes the framework for classifying accidents. This passage presents the fundamental division of accidents into stationary (qarr) and non-stationary (motion), and then further subdivides stationary accidents into relation, quantity, and quality. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping the ontological status of motion in Islamic metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Notice the logical structure: the author begins with a conditional statement dividing accidents into two types.
2
Identify the two main categories
The passage first splits accidents into stationary (qarr) and non-stationary. The non-stationary is motion. Then it splits stationary into three subcategories.
Stationary accidents are those that endure or persist.
3
Understand the criteria for subdividing stationary accidents
Stationary accidents are subdivided based on whether they are conceived with another (relation) or without another. If without another, they further divide into quantity (if they entail division) or quality (if not).
This is a conceptual classification based on how we conceive the accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : العرض إن لم يكن قاراً فهو الحركة وإن كان قاراً فإما أن لا يعقل إلا مع الغير فهو النسبة والإضافة أو يعقل بدون الغير وحينئذ إما أن يقتضي لذاته القسمة فهو الكم أو لا فهو الكيف. قالوا: وأما الحركة فالحق أنها من مقولة أن ينفعل وذهب بعضهم إلى أن مقولتي الفعل والانفعال اعتباريتان فلا تندرج الحركة فيهما. قوله : ( إن لم يكن قاراً أي لذاته فيخرج الزمان لأنَّ عدم قاريته بواسطة كونه مقداراً له والفعل والانفعال إما داخلان على ما صرح به البعض أو عدم قاريتهما لمقارنة الزمان المقارن للحركة . قوله : ( من مقولة أن ينفعل إن فسر بالخروج من القوة إلى الفعل تدريجاً وإن فسر بكمال أول لما هو بالقوة من جهة ما هو بالقوة فمن مقولة الكيف . قوله : (فالحق أنها من مقولة أن ينفعل كما يظهر من قولنا حركت الشيء فتحرك وقد. قوله : ( فلا تندرج الحركة فيها لكونها محسوسة. يقال : الحركة إن فسرت بالخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج فهي من مقولة الانفعال وإن فسرت بالتوسط فهي من مقولة الإضافة وإن فسرت بقطع المسافة فهي من مقولة الفعل وإن فسرت بالكون في آنين في مكانين أو الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني فهي من مقولة الأين .
And he said: 'The accident ('arad) — if it is not stationary (qarr) it is motion (haraka), and if it is stationary, then either it is not conceived except with another, so it is relation (nisba) or addition (idafa), or it is conceived without another — then either it necessarily brings about division (qisma) in its essence, so it is quantity (kamm), or it does not, so it is quality (kayf).' They said: 'As for motion, the truth is that it belongs to the category of being acted upon (an yanfa'il), and some held that the two categories of acting (fi'l) and being acted upon (infi'al) are conceptual (i'tibariyyan), so motion is not included in them.' His statement: 'if it is not stationary' — i.e., in its essence, so time (zaman) is excluded because its non-stationariness is due to its being a measure (miqdar) for it [motion], and acting and being acted upon are either included [as stationary] according to what some explicitly state, or their non-stationariness is due to the time concomitant to motion. His statement: 'from the category of being acted upon' — if motion is interpreted as gradual emergence from potentiality to actuality, it is from the category of being acted upon; if it is interpreted as the first perfection of that which is in potentiality insofar as it is in potentiality, then it is from the category of quality. His statement: 'the truth is that it belongs to the category of being acted upon, as appears from our saying: 'I moved something and it moved''. His statement: 'so motion is not included in them because it is sensible' (mahsub). It is said: Motion — if interpreted as gradual emergence from potentiality to actuality, it is from the category of being acted upon (al-infi'al); if interpreted as intermediateness (tawassut), it is from the category of addition (al-idafa); if interpreted as traversing distance (qat' al-masafa), it is from the category of acting (al-fi'l); if interpreted as 'being in two instants in two places' (al-kawn fi anayn fi makanayn) or 'the first 'being in the second locus'', it is from the category of position (al-ayn).
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of motion (al-haraka) and its categorical status. It begins with a general classification of accidents: if an accident is not stationary (i.e., it is non-enduring), it is motion; if it is stationary (qarr), then it falls into relation, quantity, or quality depending on how it is conceived. The passage then states the truth that motion belongs to the category of being acted upon (an yanfa'il), though some philosophers consider both the categories of acting (fi'l) and being acted upon (infi'al) to be merely conceptual (i'tibari), so motion would not be a proper category. The commentator provides key clarifications: the phrase 'if it is not stationary' refers to essence, so time (zaman) is excluded because its non-stationariness is derivative (as a measure of motion); the categories of acting and being acted upon may be considered stationary by some or non-stationary due to time. The definition of motion as 'gradual emergence from potentiality to actuality' places it in the category of being acted upon, while if defined as 'first perfection of the potential' it falls into quality. The commentator affirms that motion is indeed from the category of being acted upon, giving the linguistic example 'I moved something and it moved'. The next comment explains that some reject motion's inclusion in the two categories because motion is sensible, whereas the categories of acting and being acted upon are conceptual. Finally, a synthetic note lists alternative definitions of motion and their corresponding categories: as gradual emergence = infi'al; as intermediateness = idafa; as traversing distance = fi'l; as being in two instants in two places = ayn (position).

Correction: Motion is defined as a non-stationary accident (ghayr qarr) in this passage.

Checkpoint

A) Relation
B) Quantity
C) Motion
D) Quality

Key Takeaway
Motion is the non-stationary accident; all other accidents are stationary and belong to relation, quantity, or quality.

The Categorical Status of Motion
Al-Isfahani examines the categorical status of motion and presents multiple definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the truth is that motion belongs to the category of being acted upon (an yanfa'il), and how different definitions of motion correspond to different categories.

Core Idea
Motion's category depends on its definition; the truth is it is in the category of being acted upon.

After classifying motion as a non-stationary accident, al-Isfahani turns to the debate about which Aristotelian category motion falls into. This passage presents the position that motion is in the category of being acted upon, addresses an objection that acting and being acted upon are merely conceptual, and lists alternative definitions that would place motion in other categories. This reveals the complexity of defining motion in post-Avicennan philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
The passage begins with the claim that the truth is motion belongs to the category of being acted upon. Notice the objection that follows.
2
Understand the objection and response
Some consider fi'l and infi'al as merely conceptual (i'tibari), so motion would not be a genuine category. The commentary clarifies by linking the definitions.
3
List the different definitions and their categories
The final part enumerates five definitions of motion and assigns each to a different category. This shows that the categorical assignment depends on the definition.
Memorize the correspondences: gradual emergence → infi'al; intermediateness → idafa; traversing distance → fi'l; being in two places → ayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال : العرض إن لم يكن قاراً فهو الحركة وإن كان قاراً فإما أن لا يعقل إلا مع الغير فهو النسبة والإضافة أو يعقل بدون الغير وحينئذ إما أن يقتضي لذاته القسمة فهو الكم أو لا فهو الكيف. قالوا: وأما الحركة فالحق أنها من مقولة أن ينفعل وذهب بعضهم إلى أن مقولتي الفعل والانفعال اعتباريتان فلا تندرج الحركة فيهما. قوله : ( إن لم يكن قاراً أي لذاته فيخرج الزمان لأنَّ عدم قاريته بواسطة كونه مقداراً له والفعل والانفعال إما داخلان على ما صرح به البعض أو عدم قاريتهما لمقارنة الزمان المقارن للحركة . قوله : ( من مقولة أن ينفعل إن فسر بالخروج من القوة إلى الفعل تدريجاً وإن فسر بكمال أول لما هو بالقوة من جهة ما هو بالقوة فمن مقولة الكيف . قوله : (فالحق أنها من مقولة أن ينفعل كما يظهر من قولنا حركت الشيء فتحرك وقد. قوله : ( فلا تندرج الحركة فيها لكونها محسوسة. يقال : الحركة إن فسرت بالخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج فهي من مقولة الانفعال وإن فسرت بالتوسط فهي من مقولة الإضافة وإن فسرت بقطع المسافة فهي من مقولة الفعل وإن فسرت بالكون في آنين في مكانين أو الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني فهي من مقولة الأين .
And he said: 'The accident ('arad) — if it is not stationary (qarr) it is motion (haraka), and if it is stationary, then either it is not conceived except with another, so it is relation (nisba) or addition (idafa), or it is conceived without another — then either it necessarily brings about division (qisma) in its essence, so it is quantity (kamm), or it does not, so it is quality (kayf).' They said: 'As for motion, the truth is that it belongs to the category of being acted upon (an yanfa'il), and some held that the two categories of acting (fi'l) and being acted upon (infi'al) are conceptual (i'tibariyyan), so motion is not included in them.' His statement: 'if it is not stationary' — i.e., in its essence, so time (zaman) is excluded because its non-stationariness is due to its being a measure (miqdar) for it [motion], and acting and being acted upon are either included [as stationary] according to what some explicitly state, or their non-stationariness is due to the time concomitant to motion. His statement: 'from the category of being acted upon' — if motion is interpreted as gradual emergence from potentiality to actuality, it is from the category of being acted upon; if it is interpreted as the first perfection of that which is in potentiality insofar as it is in potentiality, then it is from the category of quality. His statement: 'the truth is that it belongs to the category of being acted upon, as appears from our saying: 'I moved something and it moved''. His statement: 'so motion is not included in them because it is sensible' (mahsub). It is said: Motion — if interpreted as gradual emergence from potentiality to actuality, it is from the category of being acted upon (al-infi'al); if interpreted as intermediateness (tawassut), it is from the category of addition (al-idafa); if interpreted as traversing distance (qat' al-masafa), it is from the category of acting (al-fi'l); if interpreted as 'being in two instants in two places' (al-kawn fi anayn fi makanayn) or 'the first 'being in the second locus'', it is from the category of position (al-ayn).
Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of motion (al-haraka) and its categorical status. It begins with a general classification of accidents: if an accident is not stationary (i.e., it is non-enduring), it is motion; if it is stationary (qarr), then it falls into relation, quantity, or quality depending on how it is conceived. The passage then states the truth that motion belongs to the category of being acted upon (an yanfa'il), though some philosophers consider both the categories of acting (fi'l) and being acted upon (infi'al) to be merely conceptual (i'tibari), so motion would not be a proper category. The commentator provides key clarifications: the phrase 'if it is not stationary' refers to essence, so time (zaman) is excluded because its non-stationariness is derivative (as a measure of motion); the categories of acting and being acted upon may be considered stationary by some or non-stationary due to time. The definition of motion as 'gradual emergence from potentiality to actuality' places it in the category of being acted upon, while if defined as 'first perfection of the potential' it falls into quality. The commentator affirms that motion is indeed from the category of being acted upon, giving the linguistic example 'I moved something and it moved'. The next comment explains that some reject motion's inclusion in the two categories because motion is sensible, whereas the categories of acting and being acted upon are conceptual. Finally, a synthetic note lists alternative definitions of motion and their corresponding categories: as gradual emergence = infi'al; as intermediateness = idafa; as traversing distance = fi'l; as being in two instants in two places = ayn (position).

كون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion belongs to only one specific category universally.
Correction: Motion's category depends on its definition; it can belong to multiple categories.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which definition of motion places it in the category of 'being acted upon' (infi'al)?
A) The first perfection of the potential
B) Gradual emergence from potentiality to actuality
C) Traversing distance
D) Being in two instants in two places

Key Takeaway
The truth is that motion belongs to the category of being acted upon, but it can be categorized differently based on different definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
أنت تصنف حركات حياتك إلى مهم وتافه وحلال وحرام. ولكن على أي أساس تصنف؟ في هذا الدرس، نرى أن الفلاسفة اختلفوا في تصنيف الحركة نفسها بسبب اختلاف التعريفات. عندما تعرف فعلاً ما بأنه 'صغير' أو 'مجرد ذنب صغير'، هل تستخدم تعريفاً يخدع نفسك ويضع الفعل في تصنيف يهون عليك أمره؟ كيف يمكنك أن تضبط تعريفاتك بميزان الشرع لا بهواك؟
?
الحق في هذا الدرس أن الحركة منقادة وفعلها انفعال — أنت لست المحرك الحقيقي. تأمل قراراً اتخذته مؤخراً وشعرت فيه أنك المسيطر. هل أقررت بأن قدرتك على الحركة والعمل هي نفسها أثر من المؤثر الحقيقي؟ ماذا سيتغير في حياتك اليومية لو عشت كل لحظة تعي أن أفعالك هي في الحقيقة انفعالات لخلق الله المستمر لك؟

Why Accidents Cannot Move: The Fifth Objective
Al-Isfahani argues that accidents cannot move from one substrate to another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why theologians argue that accidents cannot transfer from one substrate to another, and the key concept of 'mutahayyiz' (spatially located).

Core Idea
Accidents cannot transfer because transfer requires spatial location, which only bodies have.

In this passage, al-Isfahani presents the fifth objective of a section on accidents: the impossibility of their transfer. He grounds this in a consensus among rational people and a logical argument: transfer is only conceivable for spatially located entities (mutahayyiz), and accidents are not such. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to get the full meaning. Let the author’s own words set the context.
2
Understand the condition for transfer
Focus on the definition of transfer (intiqal) and the condition that it requires a spatially located entity (mutahayyiz). Ask yourself why this condition is necessary.
The passage states that transfer is 'the occurrence of a thing in a locus after having been in another'.
3
Apply the condition to accidents
Consider why accidents fail this condition. Since accidents are not bodies or spatially located, they cannot logically transfer. The consensus of rational people supports this.
Note the exception about motion being 'sensible' as a clarification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد الخامس : أن العرض لا ينتقل من محل إلى محل ] يجوز في أن العرض لا ينتقل من محل إلى محل على قياس انتقال الجسم من مكان إلى مكان وهذا حكم قد اتفق العقلاء على صحته فعند المتكلمين لأن الانتقال إنما يتصور في المتحيز وذلك لأن الانتقال هو حصول الشيء في حيز بعد أن قوله : ( فلا تندرج الحركة فيها لكونها محسوسة.
The Fifth Objective: That the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another. It is valid [to say] that the accident does not transfer from one substrate to another by analogy with the transfer of a body from one place to another, and this is a judgment whose correctness the rational people have agreed upon. For according to the theologians (al-mutakallimin), transfer is conceivable only in that which is spatially located (mutahayyiz), because transfer is the occurrence of a thing in a locus (hayyiz) after having been in another. His statement: 'so motion is not included in them because it is sensible'.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage states the fifth objective: that an accident (al-'arad) cannot transfer from one substrate (mahl) to another. It presents this as a judgment agreed upon by all rational people (al-'uqala'). The reasoning, according to the theologians (al-mutakallimin), is that transfer (intiqal) is only conceivable for something that is spatially located (mutahayyiz), i.e., a body. Transfer is defined as the occurrence of a thing in a locus after being in another. Since accidents are not spatially located, they cannot transfer. The commentary adds a clarification: motion (haraka) is not included in the two categories (acting and being acted upon) because it is sensible (mahsub), implying that motion is an accident that does not transfer as a body does.

Key Terms
عرض
انتقال
محل
متحيز
حيز
حركة
محسوسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that accidents like motion could move from one substrate to another, for example, by jumping from one body to another.
Correction: The passage argues this is impossible because transfer requires spatial location, which only bodies have. Motion is an accident that inheres in a body; it does not transfer independently.

Checkpoint
Why do the theologians argue that an accident cannot transfer from one substrate to another?
A) Because accidents are not created
B) Because transfer is only conceivable for something spatially located (mutahayyiz)
C) Because accidents are too light
D) Because Scripture prohibits it

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot transfer because transfer is a property of spatially located bodies, and accidents are not bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your anger (an accident) cannot transfer from another person to you, then who is truly responsible for its presence in your heart? How will you take ownership of your reactions starting today?
?
The passage says rational people agree that accidents do not transfer. When you blame your spiritual state on circumstances or people, are you contradicting this truth? What will you change in your thinking to align with this reality?
?
Motion is an accident that is sensible. Your prayers and good deeds are accidents in your body. Since they cannot transfer to another, what does this demand of you in terms of personal accountability before Allah?

The Existence of Accidents: Necessity and Consensus
Sanusi defines accidents and proves their dependence on a substrate by necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that accidents are known by necessity to depend on substances, and that the only dissenting views are either a denial of the obvious or a speculative obstinacy.

Core Idea
Accidents are perceived by the senses and cannot subsist by themselves.

This lesson introduces the fourth objective of Sanusi's work, where he establishes the existence of accidents (al-a‘rad). He argues that their dependence on a substrate is known by necessity through sense perception, and he refutes two rare deviations from this consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: denial, consensus, exception, proof.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two dissenting groups
Find the two groups that either deny accidents or claim they can subsist by themselves. Note how the author dismisses them.
Ibn Kaysan denied accidents entirely; Abu al-Hudhayl allowed an accidental will in no substrate.
3
Understand the proof from necessity
The author says necessity (al-darura) is sufficient. He appeals to sense perception: we perceive colors, sounds, etc., and know they do not subsist by themselves.
This is a foundational argument in kalam.
4
Reflect on the refutation of the accidental will
The author calls the claim that will subsists by itself and relates equally to God and others 'sheer obstinacy'. Understand why this is a problem.
The argument rests on the necessity of dependence on a substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الرابع: في إثبات العرض لم ينكر وجوده إلا ابن كيسان الأصم فإنه ذهب إلى أن العالم كله جواهر فالحرارة والبرودة واللون والضوء مثلاً عنده ليست عرضاً بل جوهراً ( والقائلون به ) أي بوجود العرض ( اتفقوا على أنَّه لا يقوم بنفسه إلا شرذمة ) قليلة لا يبالي بشأنهم ( كأبي الهُذيل ( العلاف ومن تبعه من البصريين ( فإنه جوز إرادة عرضية تحدث لا في محل وجعل الباري تعالى مريداً بها ) أي بتلك الإرادة ( والضرورة كافية لنا في هذين (المقامين) فإنا ندرك الأعراض من الألوان والأضواء والأصوات والطعوم والروائح والحرارة والبرودة وغيرها بحواسنا ولا نشك في أنها مما لا قيامها بنفسها ودعوى كون الإرادة قائمةً بنفسها وكون الباري مريداً بها مع استواء نسبتها إليه وإلى غيره مكابرة صريحة . قوله : ( فلا تندرج الحركة فيها لكونها محسوسة. قوله : ( في إثبات العرض أي في بيان ثبوته وتحققه وأنَّه لا يجوز قيامه بنفسه إلا أنه تركه بقرينة قوله والضرورة كافية لنا في المقامين اختصاراً وفيه إشارة إلى أن الحكم الضروري يجوز المقاصد إذا كان فيه خلاف رداً للمخالفين وأخذاً لضبع القاصرين . جعله من قوله : ( إرادة) عرضية لا يقال : إنه لا يقول بعرضيتهما لأنا نقول قد مر أن امتناع القول بالعرضية لا يجدي نفعاً بعد القول بكونها صفة حادثة فإنَّ حقيقة العرض هي الصفة الحادثة وفيه أنه يشترط في العرضية القيام أيضاً ولا قيام هاهنا فالصفة الحادثة عنده أعم من العرض قوله : (مع استواء نسبتها إليه وإلى غيره هذا ممنوع عنده فإنها صفة له تعالى عنده ولذا يوجب الحكم له دون غيره . فتدبر . يقال : الحركة إن فسرت بالخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على سبيل التدريج فهي من مقولة الانفعال وإن فسرت بالتوسط فهي من مقولة الإضافة وإن فسرت بقطع المسافة فهي من مقولة الفعل وإن فسرت بالكون في آنين في مكانين أو الكون الأول في الحيز الثاني فهي من مقولة الأين .
The Fourth Objective: On Establishing the Accident. None denied its existence except Ibn Kaysan al-Asamm, for he held that the entire world is substances; so heat, cold, color, light, for example, are not accidents but substance. And those who affirm it (i.e., the existence of the accident) agreed that it does not subsist by itself except a small group whose opinion is not regarded, like Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf and those who followed him among the Basrans, for he allowed an accidental will that occurs not in a substrate and made the Exalted Creator willing by it (i.e., by that will). And necessity is sufficient for us in these two (matters), for we perceive the accidents—colors, lights, sounds, tastes, smells, heat, cold, and others—by our senses and do not doubt that they are such that they do not subsist by themselves. And the claim that the will subsists by itself and that the Creator is willing by it while its relation to Him and to others is equal is sheer obstinacy. His saying: (So motion is not included in them because it is perceived.) His saying: (On Establishing the Accident) i.e., on demonstrating its existence and realization and that it is not permissible for it to subsist by itself, except that he omitted it by the indication of his saying 'and necessity is sufficient for us in the two objectives' for brevity. And in it there is an indication that the necessary judgment is permissible in the objectives when there is disagreement in it, as a refutation of the opponents and a taking of the side of the deficient. He made it part of his saying: (an accidental will). It cannot be said: He does not affirm their being accidental, for we say: It has passed that the impossibility of saying they are accidental is of no benefit after saying they are a temporal attribute, for the reality of the accident is the temporal attribute. And in it there is the condition that in being accidental there is also subsistence, and there is no subsistence here, so the temporal attribute according to him is more general than the accident. His saying: (while its relation to Him and to others is equal) this is denied by him, for it is an attribute of Him, exalted, according to him, and therefore it necessitates the judgment for Him to the exclusion of others. Reflect. It is said: Motion, if interpreted as the exit from potentiality to actuality gradually, then it is of the category of passion (infi‘al); if interpreted as intermediate, then it is of the category of relation (idafa); if interpreted as traversing distance, then it is of the category of action (fi‘l); if interpreted as existence in two moments in two places, or the first existence in the second space, then it is of the category of where (ayn).
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a definition and a proof concerning the existence of accidents (al-a‘rad). It begins by defining the accident as something that does not subsist by itself, and argues that its existence is known by necessity. The author first notes that only Ibn Kaysan al-Asamm denied the existence of accidents, claiming that everything is a substance. Against him, the author affirms that the majority agreed that accidents do not subsist by themselves, with a small exception: Abu al-Hudhayl allowed an accidental will that occurs not in a substrate, making God willing by it. The author dismisses this as mere obstinacy, because we perceive accidents through our senses and know necessarily that they do not subsist by themselves. The rest of the passage is commentary explaining the main text. The commentator clarifies that motion is not included among accidents because it is perceived (by reason?), and explains that the phrase 'on establishing the accident' means demonstrating its existence and that it cannot subsist by itself. He then addresses the issue of the accidental will: someone might object that Abu al-Hudhayl does not affirm accidentalness for that will, but the commentator argues that since he affirms it is a temporal attribute, and the reality of an accident is a temporal attribute, it is effectively an accident, though he distinguishes between 'temporal attribute' (more general) and 'accident' (which requires subsistence in a substrate). The commentator also refutes the idea that God's will has equal relation to Him and others, saying it is an attribute specific to Him. Finally, a note on motion defines it in various ways according to different philosophical interpretations, tying it to categories such as passion, relation, action, and where.

Key Terms
العرض (al-‘arad, accident)
الجوهر (al-jawhar, substance)
الإرادة العرضية (al-irada al-‘aradiyya, accidental will)
الضرورة (al-darura, necessity)
القيام بالنفس (al-qiyam bi al-nafs, subsistence by itself)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents can exist independently of a substance.
Correction: The passage argues that sense perception shows necessarily that accidents depend on a substance.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument for the existence of accidents according to Sanusi?
A) The consensus of the early Muslims.
B) The necessity of sense perception that accidents do not subsist by themselves.
C) The impossibility of an accident transferring.
D) The definition of motion.

Key Takeaway
Accidents are known by necessity to depend on substances, and any claim to the contrary is either a denial of the obvious or a speculative obstinacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You perceive colors, sounds, and other accidents every moment. Do you ever pause to recognize that none of these exist by themselves—they all depend on something else? How does this constant awareness of dependence train your heart to see your own utter dependence on Allah? Will you today, when you see a color or hear a sound, consciously say, 'I am not self-sufficient; You alone are the Self-Sufficient'?
?
The passage calls the claim that a will can exist without a substrate 'sheer obstinacy.' Is there any belief you hold about Allah, even subconsciously, that might be based on your own reasoning rather than on revealed truth and what is self-evident? What would it take for you to surrender that obstinacy and accept the plain truth of necessity?

The Sixth Aim: An Accident Cannot Inhere in Another Accident
Sanusi presents the sixth aim and its first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that an accident cannot inhere in another accident, based on the meaning of inherence as consequential spatialization.

Core Idea
Accidents cannot inhere in accidents because inherence requires spatial dependency on a subject with independent location.

This lesson presents the first proof for the sixth aim of kalam: that an accident cannot be the subject of another accident. The argument hinges on the definition of inherence of an attribute in its subject: the attribute's spatial location follows that of the subject. The passage explains why this makes it impossible for an accident to inhere in another accident, contrary to the philosophers, and clarifies related conditions like adjacency and contact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the central claim: 'It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident.' This is the sixth aim.
The passage also mentions the disagreement with the philosophers.
2
Understand the first proof
Focus on the first proof: the inherence of an attribute in a described thing means the attribute's spatialization (taḥayyuz) follows the spatialization of the described. Ask yourself what this implies for the possibility of an accident inhering in another accident.
The key is that an accident has no independent spatialization; it borrows spatialization from its substance-subject.
3
Analyze the explanation on preparedness and conditions
The commentary explains that adjacency and contact are conditions for preparedness, not preparers. Consider why this distinction matters for the proof.
This addresses a potential objection about how accidents interact.
4
Examine the clarification on absolute vs. specific localization
The passage ends with a clarification about the body's need for place in absolute localization, not specific localization. Reflect on how this avoids a regress.
This shows that the proof does not require the accident to have its own place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد السادس : لا يجوز قيام العرض بالعرض ] ( لا يجوز قيام العرض بالعرض عند أكثر العقلاء خلافاً للفلاسفة. لنا) في عدم الجواز (وجوه والمذكور في الكتاب وجهان (الأول) أن قيام الصفة) بالموصوف ( معناه تحيز الصفة تبعاً لتحيز الموصوف وهذا أي كون الشيء متبوعاً لتحيز غيره به قوله : ( لاستعداد يحصل إلخ) أي المجاورة والمماسة شرط لحصول الاستعداد لا أنه معد حتى يرد أنه لو كان معداً لامتنع اجتماعه مع حصول الرائحة وليس كذلك . قوله : ( تحيز الصفة تبعا إلخ) يعني أنَّ التحيز التبعي أن يكون هناك تحيز واحد قائم . قوله : ( فيقال : الجسم يحتاج في كونه متحيزاً) أي في تحيزه المطلق لا في تحيزه الخاص وإلا فلا محذور إذ اللازم حينئذ انتفاء التحيز الخاص بالانتقال .
The Sixth Aim: It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident. (It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident according to most rational people, contrary to the philosophers. We have, regarding the impermissibility, proofs, and what is mentioned in the book are two aspects. The first: That the inherence of an attribute in the described means the spatialization of the attribute as a consequence of the spatialization of the described, and this means that a thing is followed in its spatialization by another. His saying: '(For a preparedness obtained, etc.)' meaning that adjacency and contact are a condition for obtaining the preparedness, not that it is a preparer, so that it would be objected that if it were a preparer, it would be impossible for it to coexist with the occurrence of the smell, and that is not so. His saying: '(Spatialization of the attribute as a consequence, etc.)' means that the consequential spatialization is that there is one existing spatialization. His saying: '(It is said: The body needs in its being localized)' meaning in its absolute localization, not in its specific localization, otherwise there would be no problem, because then what is necessitated is the negation of the specific localization by transfer.
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth aim: that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. It begins by stating the ruling: it is not permissible according to most rational people, contrary to the philosophers. Then it mentions that there are proofs, and the book mentions two aspects. The first aspect is given: the inherence of an attribute in the described means the spatialization of the attribute as a consequence of the spatialization of the described. This means that the attribute is spatially extended by following the spatialization of its subject. The commentary explains that adjacency and contact are conditions for obtaining preparedness, not preparers themselves. It also explains that consequential spatialization means there is one existing spatialization. Finally, the commentary addresses an objection about the body needing place in its absolute localization, not specific localization; otherwise transfer would negate specific localization, which is not problematic.

Key Terms
قيام العرض بالعرض
قيام الصفة بالموصوف
تحيز الصفة تبعاً لتحيز الموصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that an accident can inhere in another accident because attributes seem to qualify other attributes.
Correction: The proof shows that inherence entails consequential spatialization, which requires the subject to have independent spatialization. Accidents lack that, so they cannot be subjects for other accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof in this passage, what does the inherence of an attribute in the described entail?
A) The attribute's spatialization is independent of the described.
B) The attribute's spatialization follows the spatialization of the described.
C) The attribute's inherence is like a substance's inherence in place.
D) The attribute does not need a subject.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for the impermissibility of an accident inhering in another accident is that inherence requires the attribute's spatialization to be dependent on the subject's spatialization, but an accident lacks independent spatialization to serve as a subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the countless qualities and experiences you have, do you ever assume they exist independently of your Creator? How does the principle that accidents cannot inhere in accidents remind you that every attribute you possess is utterly dependent on Allah, and that nothing in creation has self-sufficiency? Will you start each day by consciously acknowledging that your very existence and all your qualities are sustained moment by moment by Him?
?
Think of a situation where you attributed a change in your life to a secondary cause—like a person, circumstance, or your own effort—as if that cause were independent. How does the proof that inherence requires a subject with independent spatialization challenge that assumption? Are you willing to realign your understanding so that you see all causes as merely channels of Allah's power, not independent agents?
?
If even the spatialization of an accident is borrowed from its substance, and substances themselves are contingent, what does that imply about your own sense of permanence or stability? In what area of your life do you feel most 'secure' or 'independent'? How can you use this lesson to break that illusion and rely wholly on Allah?
